The Principle of La Talazum and Its Applications
Expansion on the la talazum principle through examples and a dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of la talazum to determine the number of expiations and divorces in various oath scenarios.

Core Idea
Ila' and ordinary oaths are independent; multiplicity in one does not imply multiplicity in the other.

The commentator introduces a crucial principle: there is no necessary correlation (la talazum) between being an ila' oath and being an ordinary oath. This means that the number of divorces triggered by ila' and the number of expiations required for violating oaths can differ. The lesson presents four examples to illustrate the possible combinations and also addresses a dispute about repeated conditional oaths, where the Hanafi school holds that the oath itself is not repeated even if the condition occurs repeatedly.

Guided Path
1
Understand La Talazum
Grasp that an oath may be considered ila' (triggering divorce) and an ordinary oath (triggering expiation) independently. Their counts can differ.
The principle is stated as 'la talazum' - no necessary correspondence.
2
Analyze Example 1 (Multiple ila', multiple oaths)
A husband swears two conditional oaths: 'if tomorrow comes, by Allah I will not approach you; if the day after tomorrow comes, by Allah I will not approach you.' Each oath is separate in both ila' and yamin. If he abstains for four months from the first day, two divorces occur (one for each ila'); if he approaches after two days, two expiations are due.
3
Analyze Example 3 (Single ila', multiple violations, single expiation)
The husband says 'whenever you enter this house, by Allah I will not approach you.' The condition is repeated, but the oath is not. The Hanafi view: the oath is a single contract; it is not repeated. Only one expiation is due even if violated multiple times. However, ila' (divorce) can be multiple because each conditional trigger creates a new ila' oath for divorce purposes.
This example causes dispute with Fath al-Qadir, which the commentator refutes.
4
Analyze Example 4 (Single ila', multiple oaths)
The husband says in one session: 'if tomorrow comes, by Allah I will not approach you; if tomorrow comes, by Allah I will not approach you.' The ila' is one (same condition), but the oaths are two (repeated formula). So if he approaches, two expiations; if he abstains until after the specified time, one divorce.
5
Resolve the Dispute on Repeated Conditional Oaths
The commentator defends the Hanafi position that the oath does not repeat despite the repeated condition. The proof: an oath is a contract (insha') and contracts are not repeated without repeating the formula. Divorce and manumission repeat because they are direct consequences of the condition, not contracts.
Fatwa collections (Walwaljiyya, Bazzaziyya) support this.

فتوى ابن  ايلاء ولو مكث يوماً ثم قال والله لا أقربك شهرين بعد الشهرين الأولين أو قال والله لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً أو قال بالبصرة والله لا أدخل مكة وهي بها لا وإن حلف ومثال الثالث كلما دخلت هذه الدار بانت بثالثة لتعدد البر، وفي فتح القدير : وفي هذا المثال نظر لأن الحلف بالله وقع جزاء لشرط متكرر فيلزم تكرره. ولا يشكل بأنه لا حلف عند الشرط الثاني والثالث لأنه لم يوجد فيه ذكر اسم الله تعالى وإلا لزم أن لا حلف عند الشرط الأول أيضاً، ومع ذلك ثبت الحلف عنده ولعله اشتبه بو الله كلما دخلت الدار لا أقربك أو بكلما دخلت الدار فوالله لا أقربك ا هـ. والجواب لا اشتباه لأن المنقول في الفتاوى كالولوالجية والبزازية أن الطلاق والعتاق والظهار متى علق بشرط متكرر يتكرر واليمين لا وإن علق بمتكرر حتى لو قال كلما دخلت الدار فوالله لا أكلم زيداً فدخل الدار مراراً لا يتكرر اليمين لأنه إنشاء عقد والإنشاء لا يتكرر بلا تكرر صيغته، ألا ترى أنه لا يتعدد وإن سمى التعدد لأن الكفارة لا تلزم بلا هتك حرمة اسم الله تعالى ا هـ . وقوله والإلزام أن لا حلف عند الشرط الأول ( ممنوع لأنه صريح قيد كما لا يخفى. ومثال الرابع أعني اتحاد الإيلاء وتعدد اليمين : إذا جاء غد فوالله لا أقربك ثم قال في المجلس إذا جاء غد فوالله لا أقربك فهو إيلاء واحد في حكم البر حتى لو مضت أربعة أشهر الغد طلقت وإن قربها فعليه كفارتان لاتحاد المدة وتعدد الاسم.
He said: (And by Allah, I will not approach you for two months and two months after these two months is ila') because combining with the conjunction 'and' is like combining with the word itself. His saying 'after these two months' is an incidental qualification because if he had not mentioned it, the ruling would be the same. It is qualified with 'and' without repeating the negation and the oath because if he repeated the negation by saying 'by Allah, I will not approach you for two months nor for two months' or repeated the oath by saying 'by Allah, I will not approach you for two months, by Allah, I will not approach you for two months', it would not be ila' because they are two oaths, so their periods overlap. If he approaches her before two months pass, two expiations are required; if he approaches after they pass, no expiation is required because the periods have expired. The ruling of an oath is like the ruling of ila' in not being multiple if only with 'and', and multiplicity if the negation or the oath is repeated. There is no difference in repeating the oath whether the object of the oath is repeated or not, even if he says 'by Allah, by Allah, I will not do such-and-such', it is two oaths in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), like his saying 'by Allah, I will not do such-and-such, by Allah, I will not do such-and-such'. Know that there is no necessary correlation between being ila' and being an oath; thus, sometimes the fulfillment (birr) and violation (hanth) may be multiple or single, or the fulfillment may be multiple and the violation single, and vice versa. The first example: If he says 'if tomorrow comes, by Allah, I will not approach you; if the day after tomorrow comes, by Allah, I will not approach you', then ila' is multiple due to multiple periods, and the oath is multiple due to multiple mention. If he abstains for four months from the first day, he fulfills the first and she is divorced; then if another day passes, he fulfills the second and she is also divorced. If he approaches her after the day after tomorrow, two expiations are required; if he approaches her on the coming tomorrow, one expiation is required. Second example: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for four months' and likewise the book's case. His saying: (Circular reasoning as in Fath al-Qadir, its text: 'The meaning mentioned is that the mu'li (one who takes the oath) is someone who cannot approach within the period except by something that is required of him, but this is not the case; it is based on the minimum period being four months, otherwise we do not say that, since we said there is no restriction of the period sworn upon by it. So establishing the minimum as four months by this is circular reasoning.') In al-Inaya he said: 'If it is said: The fatwa of Ibn Abbas contradicts the apparent meaning of the text because Allah Almighty said: "For those who swear off their wives, a waiting period of four months" [2:226]. He used ila' absolutely and restricted the waiting period to a term. This implies that whoever swears off his wife, even for a short period like a day or an hour, must wait four months. Restricting the oath to a period would be an addition to the text, which is not permissible by the fatwa of Ibn Abbas. The answer is that the fatwa of Ibn [page break] ila' and if he stays a day then said 'by Allah, I will not approach you for two months after the first two months' or said 'by Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day' or said 'in Basra, by Allah, I will not enter Mecca' while she is there, it is not [ila']? No, it is not ila' in these three cases. The third example: 'Whenever you enter this house, by Allah, I will not approach you' and she entered it on a day, then on another day, then on another day; if he approaches her, one expiation is required due to unity of violation; if he abstains from her for four months from the first day, she is divorced by one divorce; if another day passes, she is divorced by another divorce; similarly if another day passes, she is divorced by a third divorce due to multiplicity of fulfillment. In Fath al-Qadir: 'There is a problem in this example because the oath by Allah occurs as the penalty for a repeated condition, so it must be repeated. It is not problematic that there is no oath at the second and third condition because the name of Allah Almighty is not mentioned therein; otherwise, it would follow that there is no oath at the first condition either, yet the oath is established there. Perhaps it was confused with 'by Allah, whenever you enter the house, I will not approach you' or with 'whenever you enter the house, by Allah, I will not approach you'. The answer: There is no confusion because what is transmitted in the fatwa collections like al-Walwaljiyya and al-Bazzaziyya is that divorce, manumission, and zihar, whenever suspended on a repeated condition, are repeated, but the oath is not, even if suspended on a repeated condition. So if he says 'Whenever you enter the house, by Allah, I will not speak to Zayd' and she enters the house multiple times, the oath is not repeated because it is the creation of a contract, and creation is not repeated without repetition of its formula. Do you not see that it is not repeated even if multiplicity is specified because expiation is not required without violating the sanctity of the name of Allah? And his saying that the implication is that there is no oath at the first condition is rejected because it is explicitly conditional, as is not hidden. The fourth example, namely the unity of ila' and multiplicity of the oath: If he says 'if tomorrow comes, by Allah, I will not approach you' then he says in the session 'if tomorrow comes, by Allah, I will not approach you', it is one ila' in the ruling of fulfillment, so if four months of the tomorrow pass, she is divorced; if he approaches her, he owes two expiations due to unity of period and multiplicity of the name.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is an expansion probe (sharh) on the rules of multiple oaths and their relation to ila'. The passage begins by explaining that an oath combining two periods with 'and' (e.g., 'two months and two months') is considered one ila' oath. However, if the negation or the oath itself is repeated (e.g., 'by Allah, not two months nor two months' or repeating the full oath), it becomes two separate oaths, and the legal effects differ: he may owe multiple expiations. The commentator then establishes a general rule: there is no necessary correlation (la talazum) between being an ila' oath and being an ordinary oath. This means that the number of ila' oaths (which affect divorce) and the number of ordinary oaths (which affect expiation) can differ. He provides four examples to illustrate various combinations. The first example shows multiple ila' and multiple oaths. The second example shows the book's case. The third example involves a conditional oath repeated upon entry; there is a dispute with Fath al-Qadir about whether the oath repeats. The Hanafi position, based on fatwa collections, is that the oath itself does not repeat because it is an instant contract, unlike the suspended rulings (divorce, etc.). The fourth example shows unity of ila' but multiplicity of oaths. The passage also includes a digression refuting the claim that the four-month minimum is circular reasoning, citing Fath al-Qadir and al-Inaya, and affirming the fatwa of Ibn Abbas as the basis. The final part discusses cases where ila' does not occur: if he stays a day and then takes a new oath for two months after the first two months, or for a year except a day, or an oath not to enter Mecca while she is there, these are not ila'.

Key Terms
لا تلازم (la talazum): The principle that there is no necessary correspondence between being an ila' oath and being an ordinary oath; they can differ in number and effect.
يمين (yamin): An oath taken by a husband, which may or may not constitute ila' depending on the duration and wording.
إيلاء (ila'): A special oath of abstinence from marital relations for four months or more.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third example (repeated condition), why does the Hanafi school hold that the oath is not repeated?
A) Because the condition is not repeated in the wording.
B) Because the oath is a contract that is not repeated without repeating its formula.
C) Because the wife's entry does not trigger a new oath.
D) Because the husband did not intend to repeat.

Key Takeaway
The number of oaths and ila' oaths are independent; la talazum is a key principle in Hanafi jurisprudence for analyzing multiple oaths.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
8 min
Multiple Oaths in Ila': Multiplication or Unity?
Explaining rulings on multiple oaths of abstinence and resulting kaffarah/talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where multiple oaths in ila' multiply and where they do not, and apply the Hanafi rule that oaths do not multiply with repeated conditions.

Core Idea
Oaths do not multiply with repeated conditions; only repetition of the wording creates multiple oaths.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the intricate rulings of multiple oaths in ila'. The author clarifies when a husband's repeated oaths of abstinence count as one or multiple, and the consequent effects on divorce and expiation. The key distinction lies in whether the oath is a single contract (insha') or multiple.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Wording and Context
Examine the husband's exact phrasing. Does he repeat the word 'Wallahi' or does he use a conditional structure like 'kullama dakhalti hadhihi al-dar'? This determines whether we are dealing with a single oath or multiple.
The basic rule: oaths are tied to the utterance of Allah's name.
2
Classify the Type of Act
Determine whether the act is a 'yamin' (oath) or one of talaq/itaq/zihar. The Fatawa works state that talaq, emancipation, and zihar multiply with repeated conditions, but oaths do not.
The precedent is from al-Walwaljiyya and al-Bazzaziyya.
3
Apply the Rule on Multiplication
If the oath is a single suspended condition repeated (e.g., 'kullama dakhalti...'), the oath does not multiply. Only one kaffarah is due for breach, regardless of how many times the condition occurs. The passage of time for ila' is counted from the first condition.
Example: 'By Allah, whenever you enter I will not approach' – multiple entries, one oath.
4
Consider Intention and Repetition of Wording
If the wording 'Wallahi la aqrabuki' is repeated (e.g., three times in one sitting), the intention matters: if he intends repetition (takrar), the oath is one; if no intention, three oaths; if emphasis (tashdid), three oaths according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf.
Multiple sittings multiply both the ila' and the oath.
5
Note the Correction on Overlapping Periods
When the husband says 'two months after the first two months', the periods do NOT overlap. Each oath has its own separate period, so approaching in the first two months incurs only one kaffarah, not two.
Fath al-Qadir corrects the error in previous commentaries.

تتداخل المدتان، فلو قربها في الشهرين الأولين لزمته كفارة واحدة، وكذا في الشهرين الأخيرين لأنه لم يجتمع على شهرين يمينان بل على كل شهرين يمين واحدة، وقد توارد شروح الهداية من النهاية ومختصريها وغاية الجوهرة ولو كرر والله لا أقربك ثلاثاً في مجلس واحد، فإن أراد التكرار فالإيلاء واحد واليمين واحدة وإن لم يكن له نية فالإيلاء واحد واليمين ثلاث، وإن أراد التغليظ والتشديد فالإيلاء واحد واليمين ثلاث في [Chapter of Ila'] If he stays a day then says, 'By Allah, I will not approach you for two months after the first two months,' or says, 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day,' or says, 'In Basra, by Allah, I will not enter Makkah, and she is there' – no [he is not a moli]. And example of the third: 'Whenever you enter this house, by Allah, I will not approach you,' and she enters on a day, then on a day, then on another day. If he approaches her, one expiation is obligatory due to the unity of breach. If he leaves her for four months from the first day, she is separated by one divorce. When another day passes, she is separated by another divorce, and so on for a third, due to multiplicity of the oath. In Fath al-Qadir: There is consideration in this example because the oath by Allah is a penalty for a repeated condition, so repetition is required. It is not problematic that there is no oath at the second and third conditions because the name of Allah is not mentioned there, otherwise it would require that there is no oath at the first condition as well, yet the oath is established. Perhaps it is confused with 'By Allah, whenever you enter the house, I will not approach you' or 'Whenever you enter the house, by Allah, I will not approach you.' The answer: No confusion, for it is transmitted in Fatawa like al-Walwaljiyya and al-Bazzaziyya that talaq, emancipation, and zihar, when suspended on a repeated condition, are repeated, but the oath is not, even if suspended on a repeated condition. So if he says, 'Whenever you enter the house, by Allah, I will not speak to Zayd,' and he enters the house repeatedly, the oath is not repeated because it is an initiation of a contract, and initiation is not repeated without repetition of its wording. Do you not see that it does not become multiple even if multiplicity is named, because expiation is not obligatory without desecrating the sanctity of Allah's name. And his statement: 'The implication is that there is no oath at the first condition' is untenable because it is an explicit restriction as is not hidden. And the fourth example, meaning unity of ila' and multiplicity of oath: If he says, 'When tomorrow comes, by Allah, I will not approach you,' then says in the same sitting, 'When tomorrow comes, by Allah, I will not approach you,' it is one ila' in terms of oath, so if four months of tomorrow pass, she is divorced, and if he approaches her, two expiations are due due to unity of period and multiplicity of the Name.

His statement: 'If he stays a day then says, By Allah, I will not approach you for two months after the first two months, or says, By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day, or says, In Basra, by Allah, I will not enter Makkah and she is there' – no, i.e., he is not a moli in these three issues. As for the first, because the second is a new obligation, or he has become prohibited after the first oath for two months, and after the second for four except a day, so the period of prohibition was not complete. He meant by 'day' absolute time because there is no difference whether he stays a day or an hour. And his restriction with 'after the two months' is also agreed, because if he did not mention it, it would be unclear whether he is a moli, but there is a difference from another perspective: when he mentions it, the period of the second oath is determined, and when he does not, their periods become one and the second is delayed after the first. However, in the issue of the book, by the day of 'Abbas, it falls among the predetermined matters, and opinion has no entry in shari'i predetermined matters, so it is heard. No one is reported to have opposed it, so it is made an interpretation of the text, not a restriction. Or its estimation: 'Allah knows best. For those who swear abstinence from their wives, a waiting period of four months' – the first is omitted by indication of the second, so it is of the type of sufficiency. His statement: 'And the example of the third' in many copies is 'and the example of the second,' which is a distortion.

'The two periods overlap' – if he approaches her in the first two months, one expiation is incumbent on him, and similarly in the last two months, because two oaths did not combine on two months; rather, on each two months there is one oath. The commentaries on al-Hidayah, from al-Nihayah and its abridgements and Ghayat al-Bayan, have agreed on error in their discussion of this issue, so beware. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And I say: It is restricted to time because if he says absolutely: 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' then after an hour says 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' then after an hour says 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' and he approaches her after the third oath, three expiations are incumbent due to overlapping of what is sworn. If he does not approach her until four months pass, she is separated; at the completion of the second (i.e., an hour after it), she is separated by another if she is in the waiting period; and at the completion of the third, she is separated by a third without dispute. In al-Jawharah: if he repeats 'By Allah, I will not approach you' three times in one sitting, if he intends repetition, the ila' is one and the oath is one; if he has no intention, the ila' is one and the oath is three; and if he intends emphasis and intensification, the ila' is one and the oath is three according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. If the sitting is multiple, both ila' and oath are multiple. The full discussion is there.

As for the second issue, which is when he says, 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day' – the moli is one who cannot approach during the period except with something incumbent on him, and here he can approach without anything incumbent on him because the excepted day is indefinite. If he approaches her on that day, he becomes a moli when the sun sets, and he does not become a moli merely by approaching, unlike if he said 'a year except once,' for then if he approaches her, he becomes a moli instantly. It is necessary that the remainder of the year be four months or more, as al-Isbijabi mentioned. He restricted it to ila' because in rental, it is understood as the last day of the year, because it is understood as the last for its validity, for it is not valid with indefiniteness. It is not so with an oath in ila'. As for an oath in other matters, they said it is understood as the last, like his saying, 'By Allah, I will speak to so-and-so for a year except a day.' So they needed to distinguish between the two oaths. The author of al-Nihayah distinguished by the underlying meaning which is anger necessitating non-speech at present, but this is open to objection because it is common to both, since ila' also can be due to anger. Thus in Fath al-Qadir following the commentator. And it may be said that it is not necessary in ila' to be due to anger, as when it is with her consent fear of [؟] hidden from him. That is the meaning intended by the muhaqqiq: the condition itself, i.e., the very entry, not the utterance. His statement: 'In the issue of the book, the two periods overlap.' Thus in al-Fath, and apparently the correct view is that they do not overlap, as indicated by what precedes and follows. Consider. His statement: 'If he approaches her in the first two months (etc.)' – al-Nahr said: If he approaches her in the first two months in the issue of the book, one expiation is incumbent on him, and the commentators on al-Hidayah concur that by approach two expiations are incumbent. Al-Fath said it is an error because two oaths did not combine on two months; rather, on each two months there is one oath. Since each oath has a separate period, there is no overlap between the two periods such that two expiations would be incumbent, unless by 'approach' it is meant in both periods. Thus in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah. In my view, this interpretation is something that must be accepted. This is known from his statement in al-'Inayah: 'His speech is two independent oaths; by approach two expiations are incumbent.' And you may consider the article 'al' in 'al-qurban' to be for the genus. His statement: 'And it may be said that it is not necessary in ila' etc.' al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: The objection against him is... consider.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, deals with the intricate rulings of multiple oaths in ila' (the vow of abstinence). The author, Ibn Nujaym, is explaining the legal effects of different wordings of the oath of abstinence, particularly when a husband makes repeated oaths. The passage is a typical expansion_probe (sharh) where the commentator expands on the matn (base text) and critiques previous commentaries like al-Hidayah's glosses. It falls under the category of intra_hanafi_dispute because we see differences between Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf on one point and a critique of some Hanafi commentators.

The first part of the passage gives three examples where the husband is NOT considered a moli (one who has taken an oath of abstinence). The first is when he says: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for two months after the first two months.' The reason is that the second oath starts a new term, and the total period of prohibition is not consecutive four months. The second example is 'a year except a day'—he can approach her on that day without being a moli instantly, but becomes one after sunset. The third is swearing not to enter Makkah while she is there—this is not ila' because ila' is specific to sexual abstinence. The passage then discusses a more complex example: 'Whenever you enter this house, by Allah, I will not approach you.' Here, if the wife enters multiple times, each entry is a separate condition, but the oath is one because it is a single oath suspended on a repeated condition. According to the Hanafi position from the Fatawa works, oaths do not multiply with repeated conditions (unlike talaq, emancipation, and zihar). Therefore, if he approaches her after multiple entries, only one kaffarah is due for the breach. However, regarding the passage of time for ila', each entry triggers a new period? Actually, the text says that if he leaves her for four months from the first day, she becomes separated by one talaq; then when another day passes, she is separated by another talaq (since after the first bainunah, she would be in her iddah, and if another four months pass, another talaq? The text says 'thalaathah'—three divorces). This is because the oath is considered renewed with each entry? The author explains that in Fath al-Qadir, there is a consideration because the oath is a penalty for a repeated condition, so it should repeat. But the response is that in Hanafi fiqh, oaths (yamin) do not multiply; they are like contracts (insha') that require repetition of the formula. This is a key distinction between talaq/itaq/zihar (which multiply) and oaths (which do not).

The passage then presents the fourth example: unity of ila' but multiplicity of oath. If he says in one sitting, 'When tomorrow comes, by Allah, I will not approach you' twice, it is one ila' regarding the term (so if four months of 'tomorrow' pass, she is divorced), but if he approaches, two kaffarahs are due because the oath is multiple (two different utterances).

A significant part of the passage critiques the commentators on al-Hidayah who wrongly said that in the first example (two months after two months) if he approaches in the first two months, two kaffarahs are due. The correct view is that only one kaffarah is due because each oath covers its own period separately; there is no overlap. Similarly, if he approaches in the last two months, one kaffarah. This is a correction by Fath al-Qadir.

The passage then discusses repeated oaths in one sitting vs. multiple sittings. If he repeats 'By Allah, I will not approach you' three times in one sitting, the rules vary by intent: if he intends repetition (takrar), then ila' and oath are both one; if no intention, ila' one, oath three; if intent is emphasis (tashdid), then according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, ila' one, oath three (so three kaffarahs if breached). For multiple sittings, both ila' and oath multiply.

Finally, there is a detailed discussion of 'sana illa yawman' (a year except a day). The key point is that in ila', the exception is interpreted as an indefinite day, so the husband can have intercourse on that day without becoming a moli immediately; the moli status kicks in after sunset. This differs from general oaths (e.g., swearing to speak to someone) where the exception is understood to be the last day to validate the oath. The author provides the reasoning: in ila', the rule is to prevent the husband from abstaining for more than four months, so a loose interpretation that allows intercourse earlier is preferred; in other oaths, the exception is taken as the last day to make the oath valid (since indefinite exception would invalidate the oath).

The last part discusses whether the two periods in the first example overlap. The text initially says 'tadakhul al-muddatan' (the two periods overlap) but then corrects that the correct view is they do not overlap (la tadakhul). This is clarified by al-Fath and others.

Overall, this passage demonstrates the meticulousness of Hanafi fiqh in distinguishing between different types of multiple oaths and their effects on ila', divorce, and expiation.

Key Terms
إيلاء (ila'): A husband's oath to abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife for four months or more.
حنث (hanth): Breach of the oath; if the husband has intercourse in violation of the oath.
بر (birr): Fulfillment of the oath by not having intercourse for the specified period.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath; typically feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave.
اليمين (al-yamin): The oath itself; in the context, it refers to the specific wording invoking Allah's name.
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; in ila', if the husband does not resume intercourse within four months, a revocable divorce occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day.' He has intercourse on that day. When does he become a moli?
A) Immediately upon intercourse
B) At sunset of that day
C) After four months from that day
D) He is never a moli

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are contracts that do not multiply without repetition of wording, while talaq, itaq, and zihar multiply with repeated conditions.

Overlapping Periods and Multiple Oaths in Ila'
Rulings on overlapping ila' periods and multiple oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on multiple ila' oaths concerning expiation and divorce, distinguishing between overlapping periods and consecutive oaths.

Core Idea
Multiple periods in ila' do not combine for expiation; each period stands alone, and intention determines the number of oaths in one sitting.

Ila' involves a husband vowing to abstain from marital relations. When multiple such oaths are made, the timing and the husband's intention affect how many expiations are due and when divorce occurs. This lesson clarifies these rulings, warning against common errors found in earlier commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine whether the oaths cover overlapping periods, are made sequentially at intervals, or are uttered in one sitting.
Each scenario has different consequences.
2
Apply the ruling on overlapping periods
If the husband makes two oaths for different two-month blocks, only one expiation is due for each block, not combined.
3
Analyze sequential oaths
If oaths are made at intervals (e.g., hours apart), each oath carries its own expiation, and each period triggers a divorce if not broken.
Divorce becomes irrevocable after the fourth month overall.
4
Determine effect of intention in one sitting
For three oaths in one sitting, the number of oaths depends on the husband's intention: repetition (one oath), no intention (three), emphasis (three according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
There is an ikhtilaf on the emphasis scenario.
5
Recognize mujtahid error
Be aware that some earlier commentators made mistakes on these rulings, as noted by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتداخل المدتان، فلو قربها في الشهرين الأولين لزمته كفارة واحدة، وكذا في الشهرين الأخيرين لأنه لم يجتمع على شهرين يمينان بل على كل شهرين يمين واحدة، وقد توارد شروح الهداية من النهاية ومختصريها وغاية البيان على الخطأ عند كلامهم على هذه المسألة فاحذره؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وأقول: وقيد بالوقت لأنه لو أطلق بأن قال والله لا أقربك ثم قال بعد ساعة والله لا أقربك ثم بعد ساعة قال والله لا أقربك فقربها بعد اليمين الثالثة لزمه ثلاث كفارات لتداخل المحلوف عليه، ولو لم يقربها حتى مضت أربعة أشهر بانت، وعند تمام الثانية وهو ساعة بعدها تبين بأخرى إذا كانت في العدة، وعند تمام الثالثة تبين بثالثة بلا خلاف. وفي الجوهرة: ولو كرر والله لا أقربك ثلاثاً في مجلس واحد، فإن أراد التكرار فالإيلاء واحد واليمين واحدة، وإن لم يكن له نية فالإيلاء واحد واليمين ثلاث، وإن أراد التغليظ والتشديد فالإيلاء واحد واليمين ثلاث في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وإذا تعدد المجلس تعدد الإيلاء واليمين. وتمامه فيها.
The two periods overlap. If he has intercourse with her in the first two months, one expiation is incumbent upon him, and likewise in the last two months, because two oaths do not combine for two months, but rather for each two months there is one oath. The commentaries of al-Hidayah, from al-Nihayah and its abridgements and Ghayat al-Bayan, have concurred on an error in their discussion of this issue, so beware of it; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And I say: He restricted it by the time because if he made it absolute by saying, 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' then after an hour said, 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' then after an hour said, 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' then he approached her after the third oath, three expiations are incumbent upon him due to the overlapping of the thing sworn. And if he did not approach her until four months passed, she becomes irrevocably divorced. At the completion of the second (i.e., an hour after it), she becomes divorced by another (revocable) if she is in the waiting period, and at the completion of the third, she becomes divorced by a third, without disagreement. And in al-Jawharah: If he repeated 'By Allah, I will not approach you' three times in one sitting, if he intended repetition, then the ila' is one and the oath is one; if he had no intention, then the ila' is one and the oath is three; if he intended intensification and emphasis, then the ila' is one and the oath is three according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And if the sitting is multiple, the ila' and the oath become multiple. And its completion is in it.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a key issue in the chapter of Ila': the overlapping of periods when multiple oaths are made. The author first clarifys that if a man makes two separate oaths for different two-month periods, and he has intercourse within one of those periods, only one expiation is due. This is because each oath has its own timeframe, and they do not combine. He warns that earlier commentators on al-Hidayah made an error on this point, so one must be cautious. Next, he explains the case of three oaths made at intervals: if each oath is made after a short delay (e.g., an hour), and the husband approaches after the third, three expiations are due. If he does not approach at all during the four months, she becomes irrevocably divorced. At the completion of each period, a revocable divorce occurs if she is in the waiting period. Then he quotes al-Jawharah regarding three oaths in one sitting: the ruling depends on intention. If he intended merely to repeat, it is a single oath; if no intention, it is three oaths; if he intended emphasis, it is three oaths according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. If the oaths are repeated in separate sittings, both the ila' and the oath are multiple.

Key Terms
تداخل المدتان (tadākhul al-muddatayn): The overlapping of two periods of Ila' such that they share the same timeframe.
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period exceeding four months.

Key Takeaway
The number of expiations in multiple ila' oaths depends on whether periods overlap, whether oaths are sequential, and the husband's intention in a single sitting.

Oath of Abstinence for a Year Except One Day
Ruling on Ila' with an indefinite exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath of 'a year except one day' constitutes Ila', and explain the difference between such an oath and an oath of 'a year except once'.

Core Idea
An oath for a year except one day does not make the husband a moli immediately, only after intercourse and sunset of that day, provided at least four months remain.

In Hanafi fiqh, the oath of Ila' (vowing to abstain from intercourse) has specific conditions. When a husband swears to abstain for a year except one day, the indefinite exception creates a nuance: he is not immediately a moli because he can potentially fulfil the oath by having intercourse on the excepted day. Only if he actually has intercourse does he become a moli at sunset. The passage from Bahr al-Raiq unpacks this ruling and contrasts it with other oaths and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Read the passage and note the two oaths: 'a year except one day' and 'a year except once'. Understand that the excepted element is indefinite (nakirah).
2
Determine when Ila' begins
For 'a year except one day', the husband is not a moli merely by swearing. He becomes a moli only if he has intercourse on a day, and then only at sunset of that day. For 'a year except once', he becomes a moli immediately upon intercourse.
The key is whether the exception is a single indefinite day or a single occasion.
3
Check the remaining period
For the oath to constitute Ila', the remainder of the year after subtracting the excepted day must be four months or more, as per al-Asbijabi.
If the remaining period is less than four months, it is not Ila'.
4
Distinguish from other contexts
Note that in hiring (ijarah), 'except one day' refers to the last day for validity. In other oaths (e.g., 'I will not speak to him for a year except one day'), it also refers to the last day. The author of al-Nihayah attempted to distinguish Ila' by the motive of provocation, but this is criticized.
The distinction between Ila' and other oaths is based on the nature of the obligation and the possibility of immediate fulfilment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثانية وهو ما إذا قال والله لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً فإن المولى من لا يمكنه القربان في المدة إلا بشيء يلزمه ويمكنه ها هنا القربان من غير شيء يلزمه لأن المستثنى يوم منكر ولو قربها في يوم صار مولياً إذا غربت الشمس من اليوم ولا يكون مولياً بمجرد القربان بخلاف قوله سنة إلا مرة فإنه إذا قربها صار مولياً من ساعته، ولا بد فيها من كون الباقي من السنة أربعة أشهر فأكثر ذكره الأسبيجابي. قيد بالإيلاء لأن في الإجارة ينصرف إلى اليوم الأخير من السنة لأن الصرف إلى الأخير لتصحيحها فإنها لا تصح مع التنكير ولا كذلك اليمين في الإيلاء، وأما اليمين في غيره فقالوا ينصرف إلى الأخير كقوله والله أكلم فلاناً سنة إلا يوماً فاحتاجوا إلى الفرق بين اليمينين. وفرق صاحب النهاية بأن المعنى الحامل وهو المغايظة المقتضية لعدم كلامه في الحال منظور فيه بأنه مشترك الإلزام إذ الإيلاء أيضاً يكون عن المغايظة؛ كذا في فتح القدير تبعاً للشارح.
As for the second: it is when he says, 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except one day.' Then the moli is he who is unable to have intercourse during the period except by something that incurs an obligation upon him, and here he is able to have intercourse without anything incuring an obligation because the excepted day is indefinite. If he has intercourse with her on a day, he becomes a moli when the sun sets on that day, and he does not become a moli merely by intercourse, unlike his saying 'a year except once,' for then when he has intercourse, he becomes a moli immediately. And it is necessary that the remainder of the year be four months or more; al-Asbijabi mentioned it. He restricted it to Ila' because in hiring it refers to the last day of the year, because the reference to the last is for its validity, for it is not valid with indefiniteness, and it is not so for the oath in Ila'. As for the oath in other matters, they said it refers to the last, like his saying, 'By Allah, I will speak to so-and-so for a year except one day,' so they needed a distinction between the two oaths. The author of al-Nihayah distinguished by the underlying meaning which is provocation requiring that he does not speak to him immediately; but it is open to objection because it is common in obligation since Ila' also arises from provocation; thus in Fath al-Qadir following the commentator.

Commentary
This passage discusses the second scenario: when a husband swears to abstain for a year except one day. The key point is that since the excepted day is indefinite (nakirah), he can potentially have intercourse on that day without incurring any obligation (like expiation). Therefore, he is not a moli immediately. However, if he actually has intercourse on a day, he becomes a moli at sunset of that day, because the oath is now violated. This is contrasted with the phrase 'a year except once,' where intercourse immediately makes him a moli. Additionally, for this oath to constitute Ila', at least four months must remain after subtracting one day. The author then contrasts this with the rule in hiring (ijarah), where 'except one day' refers to the last day for the contract to be valid. In oaths for other matters, such as swearing not to speak for a year except one day, the reference is also to the last day. The commentator al-Nihayah tried to distinguish based on the motive (provocation), but this is criticized because Ila' can also be due to provocation.

Key Terms

الإيلاء (al-Ila'): A husband's oath to abstain from intercourse with his wife for four months or more, which can lead to divorce.

The Second Type of Ila': Moral Ila' (Al-Īlā' al-Ma'nawī)
Ruling with explanation and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition and examples of moral Ila' and determine when a conditional oath constitutes Ila' versus when it does not.

Core Idea
Moral Ila' is any conditional obligation that prevents intercourse for four months.

In this brief passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author introduces a second type of Ila' that goes beyond direct oaths. Here, the husband suspends his oath on an act of worship or similar obligation, such as Hajj or divorce. Understanding this type is crucial because it expands the scope of Ila' and prevents husbands from circumventing its effects through creative wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that if a husband swears by Hajj, fasting, emancipation, charity, divorce, or makes Ila' from a revocably divorced woman, he is considered a moli (one who makes Ila'). Thus, the ruling is that this type of conditional oath triggers Ila'.
This is the second type of Ila', termed 'moral Ila'.
2
Understand the Basis
The author explains that this type suffices for Ila' because the husband binds himself with an obligation that prevents intercourse for at least four months. The fear of having to perform a heavy duty (like Hajj) deters him from approaching his wife.
The key is that the conditional act creates a barrier to intercourse.
3
Examine the Exception
The passage addresses an exception: if the husband says 'except a day I will approach you therein,' he is not a moli. Why? Because the excepted day is indefinite—it could be any day of the year—so he can fulfill the exception within four months without incurring the obligation.
The exception must be specific and realizable within the four-month period.
4
Analyze the Reasoning
The author dismisses the objection raised by the exception as 'very little' and not a basis for a general ruling. The reasoning is that the indefinite exception allows the husband to avoid Ila' by approaching on that day, but such a rare scenario does not undermine the rule.
The default is that moral Ila' is established unless the exception is definite and immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن حلف بحج أو صوم أو عتق أو صدقة أو طلاق أو آلى من المطلقة الرجعية فهو مول) هذا شروع في القسم الثاني من الإيلاء وهو الإيلاء المعنوي وهو اليمين بتعليق ما يكفي في كونه يكون ولو في بعض المواد فكيف وهو أكثرها، وما ذكرها، وما ذكره من خوف غيل ونحوه أقل قليل لا يبنى على مثله حكم. قوله : (وبين وصفه بقوله إلا يوماً أقربك فيه (الخ إنما لم يكن مولياً لأنه استثنى يوماً منكراً فيصدق على كل يوم من أيام تلك السنة حقيقة فيمكنه قربانها قبل مضي أربعة أشهر من غير شيء.
His saying: 'And if he swears by Hajj, fasting, emancipation, charity, divorce, or makes Ila' from a revocably divorced woman, he is a moli.' This is the beginning of the second type of Ila', which is moral Ila', and it is the oath by suspending it on that which suffices for it to be, even in some respects, how much more when it is most of it, and whatever he mentioned of fear of ghayl and the like is very little; a ruling is not built on the like of it. His saying: 'And between describing it by saying 'except a day I will approach you therein'' etc. He is not a moli only because he excepted an indefinite day, so it applies to every day of that year in reality, so he can approach her before the lapse of four months without anything.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of Ila', termed 'moral Ila' (al-īlā' al-ma'nawī). This occurs when the husband makes his oath conditional upon performing an act like Hajj, fasting, manumission, charity, or divorce. For example, 'If I approach you, I must perform Hajj' or 'my wife is divorced.' The author emphasizes that this type is sufficient to constitute Ila' because it binds the husband with an obligation that prevents intercourse for at least four months. He then addresses an objection: if the husband says 'except a day I will approach you therein,' he is not a moli because the excepted day is indefinite, allowing him to approach within four months without incurring an obligation. The author dismisses the objection as rare and not basis for a general ruling.

Key Terms
الإيلاء المعنوي (al-īlā' al-ma'nawī): A type of Ila' where the oath is tied to an act of worship or similar obligation, making it binding as a vow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is an example of moral Ila' that makes the husband a moli?
A) He says 'I will not approach you for one year' without any oath.
B) He says 'If I approach you, I will give $5 to charity.'
C) He says 'If I approach you, I will perform Hajj.'
D) He says 'I will not approach you except on Fridays.'

Key Takeaway
Moral Ila' is a valid form of Ila' when the husband conditions intercourse on a serious obligation that deters him for four months.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced

Conceptual Ila' and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Future Slaves
Definition and expansion of conceptual Ila' with intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of 'burdensome act' to determine whether an oath constitutes Ila', and explain why Abu Yusuf differs from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on the case of future slaves.

Core Idea
An oath becomes Ila' if approaching requires a burdensome act, even if the burden is only possible, not certain.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi concept of 'conceptual Ila'' (al-ila' al-ma'nawi), where the husband's oath is by suspending a burdensome obligation upon consummation. The central criterion is whether fulfilling the oath is burdensome (yastashiqquhu). The commentator then examines a disputed case: if a man swears 'if I approach you, every slave I buy is free,' does he become a muli? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes, but Abu Yusuf disagrees. We will walk through the reasoning and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act in question: the husband takes an oath that suspends a burdensome act upon consummation. The legal effect is that he becomes a 'muli' (one who has made Ila').
The passage distinguishes between literal Ila' (by Allah's name) and conceptual Ila' (by suspending a burden).
2
Locate the disputed case
Focus on the oath: 'If I approach you, then every slave I buy is free.' The passage states that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the husband becomes a muli.
This is the specific case of a future, unspecified slave.
3
Trace the Hanafi reasoning (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad)
The burden exists because ownership may occur without the husband's choice (e.g., by inheritance or gift). Therefore, he cannot entirely avoid the obligation by simply refraining from purchasing.
The criterion is the possibility of burden, not certainty.
4
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that the husband can avoid the burden by not buying any slaves. Thus, there is no immediate burden, and he does not become a muli.
This reflects a stricter view on what constitutes a 'burden'.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If Abu Yusuf's view is followed, the oath does not trigger Ila', so the husband is not subject to the four-month waiting period and potential divorce. The mufta bih (relied-upon position) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This highlights the importance of knowing which position is operative for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الإيلاء
أو صوم أو صدقة أو عتق أو طلاق أو آلى من المطلقة الرجعية فهو مول ومن المبانة نقصان [؟] كذا على ولدها وعدم موافقة مزاجهما ونحوه فيتفقان عليه لقطع لجاج النفس كما صرح به في فتح القدير أول الباب ولم يتنبه له هنا وتأجيل الدين كالإجارة وقيد باليوم لأنه لو قال إلا انصرف إلى الأخير لأن النقصان منها لا يكون إلا من آخرها عرفاً والتقييد بالسنة يوم اتفاقي لأنه لو أطلق فقال لا أقربك إلا يوماً لا يكون مولياً أيضاً لكن إذا قربها هنا صار مولياً مطلقاً، وكذا لا فرق بين الاقتصار على اليوم وبين وصفه بقوله إلا يوماً أقربك فيه في كونه لا يكون مولياً لكن هنا لا يصير مولياً أبداً قربها أو لا بخلاف ما تقدم. وقيد بالاستثناء لأنه لو قال لا أقربك سنة كان مولياً ووقع عليه طلقتان فقط إذا تركها السنة كلها ولا تقع الثالثة ؛ في الولوالجية. وأما المسألة الثالثة وهو ما إذا كان في بلدة وامرأته في أخرى فحلف لا يدخل البلدة التي هي فيها لأنه يمكنه القربان من غير شيء يلزم بالإخراج من البلد بوكيله أو نائبه قبل مضي المدة، فإن كان لا يمكنه بأن كان بينهما ثمانية أشهر صار مولياً على ما في جوامع الفقه، وأما على ذكره قاضيخان فالعبرة لأربعة أشهر والذي يظهر ضعفه لإمكان خروج كل منهما إلى الآخر فيلتقيان في أقل من ذلك وقدمنا بعض مسائل الإيلاء المغيا بغاية عن الجوهرة. وفي الجامع للصدر الشهيد الغاية كالشرط قال لا أقربك حتى أقتل أو تقتلي أو أقتلك أو تقتليني أو أملكك أو تملكيني أو ما دام النكاح بيننا فهو ومول، وحتى أشتريك لا خلافاً فالزفر دليله التعليق ولو قال حتى أعتق عبدي أو أطلق امرأتي صار مولياً خلافاً لأبي يوسف. ولو قال حتى أقتله أو أضربه أو يأذن لي لا لإمكان الغاية فإن وحدت الغاية سقطت اليمين، وكذا إن تعذرت عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف وهي معروفة. ولو قال حتى أقتلك أو فلاناً وقتله بطلت وإن مات صار مولياً بعده ولو قال حتى تموت أو يموت ومات بطلت. قال في رجب لا أقربك حتى أصوم شعبان فأفطر أول منه أو عمل ما لا يوم معه الصوم بطلت يمينه وعند أبي يوسف يصير مولياً من وقت التعذر، عند محمد من وقت اليمين وخالف أصله ولو قال حتى أصوم المحرم فهو مول بالاتفاق، وكذا حتى تخرج الدابة أو تطلع الشمس من مغربها اهـ.

ذلك يمضي هذا يستشقه على القربان كإن قربتك فلله علي حج. وخرج اليمين بما لا يستشقه كإن قربتك فلله علي صلاة ركعتين أو فلله علي صلاة ركعتين في بيت المقدس لأنه لا يلزمه بتعيين المكان شيء عندنا فله صلاتهما في غيره كما خرج فعلي اتباع جنازة أو سجدة تلاوة أو قراءة القرآن أو تسبيحة، ودخل ما لو قال فلله علي مائة ركعة لأنه يشق على النفس كما في فتح القدير بحثاً. وإطلاق أن الصلاة مما لا يستشقه كما فعل الشارح مما لا ينبغي ؛ هذا إن علل الصلاة بما لا يستشق، أما إذا علل بأن الصلاة لا يحلف بها عادة كما في شرح المجمع للمصنف قال ؛ فالتحق بصلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة فلا فرق بين الركعتين ومائة ركعة كما لا يخفى. ودخل الهدي والاعتكاف واليمين وكفارة اليمين وذبح الولد لأنه يلزمه بالنذر به ذبح شاة عندنا كما في البدائع. وأراد بالصوم غير المعين كقوله فلله علي صوم يوم أو شهر، والمعين إن كان بمدة الإيلاء أو أكثر كقوله فلله علي صوم أربعة أشهر أولها هذا الشهر مثلاً، وأما إذا كان بأقل منها كقوله فلله علي صوم هذا الشهر فليس بمول لأنه يمكنه ترك القربان إلى أن ثم يطأها بلا شيء يلزمه وأطلق العتق فشمل عتق العبد المعين كقوله فلله علي عتق العبد وغيره كقوله فلله علي عتق عبد سواء كان منجزاً أو معلقاً حتى لو قال فكل مملوك اشتريته فهو حر صار مولياً خلافاً لأبي يوسف. كما أطلق الطلاق فشمل طلاقها وطلاق غيرها منجزاً أو معلقاً حتى لو قال فكل امرأة أتزوجها من أهل الإسلام طالق صار مولياً. وفي التلخيص من باب الإيلاء يكون في موطنين وفي إن قربتك فأنت طالق كلما دخلت فليس بمول لأن له مدفعاً بالترك أو بحمل الغير بخلاف فكل مملوك أملك حراً وأخر الجزاء كان مولياً للإعراض .اهـ. ومن باب الفيء في اليمين : قال إن قربتك فعبداي حران فباع أحدهما ثم اشتراه وباع الآخر أو قدم بيعه فهو مول من وقت شرائه وفي فأحدهما حر من وقت اليمين اهـ. ولو باء العبد المعين سقط الإيلاء لأنه صار بحال يمكنه قربانها بغير شيء يلزمه، ولو ملكه بسبب شراء أو غيره عاد الإيلاء من وقت الملك إن لم يكن وطئها قبله، فإن كان وطئها قبل تجدد الملك لم يعد لسقوط الإيلاء، ولو مات العبد المعين قبل البيع سقط الإيلاء لقدرته على الوطء بغير شيء، وعلى هذا التفصيل موت المرأة المعلق طلاقها أو إبانتها ثم تزوجها. وفي الجامع للصدر : قال أنت طالق ثلاثاً قبل أن أقربك بشهر أو قبل أن أقربك
يلزمه قوله : (وقيد بالاستثناء لأنه لو قال الخ) عبارة الولوالجية : رجل قال لامرأته والله لا أقربك سنة فمضى الأربعة الأشهر فبانت ثم تزوجها ومضى أربعة أشهر أخرى بانت أيضاً فإن تزوجها ثالثاً لا يقع لأنه بقي من السنة بعد التزوج أقل من أربعة أشهر.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Ila'. Or fasting, charity, freeing, divorce, or if he makes Ila' from the revocably divorced woman, he is a muli, and from the bain [irrevocably divorced] [?] like this regarding her child and lack of compatibility of their temperaments and the like, so they agree upon it to cut off the stubbornness of the self, as stated in Fath al-Qadeer at the beginning of the chapter, and he was not aware of it here. Deferment of debt is like leasing. And it is restricted by the day because if he said 'except' it refers to the last, because the deficiency from it only occurs from its end customarily. And restriction by the year is a day accidentally because if he said absolutely 'I will not approach you except for a day', he is not a muli either; however if he approaches her here, he becomes a muli absolutely. Similarly, there is no difference between restricting to the day and describing it by his saying 'except a day I approach you in it' in that he is not a muli, but here he never becomes a muli whether he approaches or not, contrary to the previous. And it is restricted by the exception because if he said 'I will not approach you for a year', he is a muli, and only two divorces occur if he leaves her the whole year, and the third does not occur; in al-Walwalijiyya. As for the third issue, which is if he is in a town and his wife in another, and he swore not to enter the town where she is, because he can approach without anything necessitating removal from the city by his agent or deputy before the period passes; if he cannot, for example there were eight months between them, he becomes a muli according to what is in Jami' al-Fiqh. And according to what Qadikhan mentioned, the consideration is for four months, and it appears weak because each can go out to the other and meet in less than that. And we have previously presented some issues of Ila' with a time limit from al-Jawhara. And in al-Jami' li al-Sadr al-Shahid, the time limit is like a condition: he said 'I will not approach you until I kill, or you kill me, or I kill you, or you kill me, or I possess you, or you possess me, or as long as marriage is between us' – he is a muli. And 'until I buy you' – no difference, for al-Zafar his proof is the ta'liq. And if he said 'until I free my slave or divorce my wife', he becomes a muli contrary to Abu Yusuf. And if he said 'until I kill him or beat him or he permits me' – no, because the time limit is possible; if the time limit occurs alone, the oath falls; similarly if it becomes impossible according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, and it is known. And if he said 'until I kill you or so-and-so' and he killed, it becomes void; and if he died, he becomes a muli after him. And if he said 'until you die or he dies' and he died, it becomes void. He said in Rajab: 'I will not approach you until I fast Sha'ban' and he broke fast at its beginning, or he did an action with which fasting is not possible – his oath becomes void. And according to Abu Yusuf, he becomes a muli from the time of impossibility; according to Muhammad, from the time of the oath, and he contradicted his principle. And if he said 'until I fast Muharram', he is a muli by agreement. Similarly, 'until the beast emerges or the sun rises from its west' etc. [؟] He is able. His statement: (And if he swears by Hajj, fasting, freeing, charity, divorce, or makes Ila' from the revocably divorced woman, he is a muli) This is the beginning of the second section of Ila', which is conceptual Ila', and it is the oath by suspending something that suffices for it to be considered such even in some cases, how much more when it is most of it? And what he mentioned of fear of gheel etc. is very little; a ruling is not built upon the like of it. His statement: (And between describing it by his saying 'except a day I approach you in it' etc.) He was not a muli because he excepted an indefinite day, so it applies truthfully to every day of that year, so he can approach her before four months pass without anything. Book of Divorce, Chapter of Ila'. That passes, this is burdensome upon approach, like 'if I approach you, then upon me is Hajj'. And the oath is excluded by what is not burdensome, like 'if I approach you, then upon me is two rak'ahs of prayer' or 'then upon me is two rak'ahs of prayer in Bayt al-Maqdis' because specifying the place does not obligate anything with us, so he can pray them elsewhere; similarly excluded is 'then upon me is following a funeral' or 'prostration of recitation' or 'reciting Quran' or 'a tasbiha'. And included is what if he said 'then upon me is one hundred rak'ahs' because it is burdensome upon the self, as investigated in Fath al-Qadeer. And the absolute statement that prayer is not burdensome, as the commentator did, is not proper. This is if he reasoned prayer as not burdensome; but if he reasoned that prayer is not customarily sworn by, as in Sharh al-Majma' by the author, he said: then it is attached to funeral prayer and prostration of recitation, so there is no difference between two rak'ahs and one hundred rak'ahs, as is not hidden. And included are hady (sacrificial animal), i'tikaf, the oath, kaffarat al-yameen, and slaughtering a child because he obligates with the vow to slaughter a sheep with us, as in al-Bada'i'. And he intended by fasting unspecified, like his saying 'then upon me is a fast of a day or a month'; and specified, if it is equal to the period of Ila' or more, like his saying 'then upon me is a fast of four months, the first of which is this month for example'; but if it is less than that, like his saying 'then upon me is a fast of this month', then he is not a muli because he can leave the approach until then and then have intercourse with her without anything obligatory upon him. And he made freeing absolute, so it includes freeing a specific slave, like his saying 'then upon me is freeing the slave', and other than that like his saying 'then upon me is freeing a slave', whether it is immediate or suspended, so that even if he says 'every slave I buy is free', he becomes a muli contrary to Abu Yusuf. Similarly, he made divorce absolute, so it includes divorcing her and divorcing other than her, immediate or suspended, so that even if he says 'every woman I marry from the people of Islam is divorces', he becomes a muli. And in al-Talkhis from the chapter of Ila': it occurs in two contexts: and in 'if I approach you, then you are divorces every time I enter', he is not a muli because he has a way out by refraining or by carrying another person, contrary to 'every slave I own is free' and he deferred the consequence – he is a muli due to disregard, etc. And from the chapter of fay' in the oath: he said 'if I approach you, then my two slaves are free' and he sold one then bought it and sold the other, or advanced their sale, then he is a muli from the time of purchase; and in 'then one of them is free', from the time of the oath, etc. And if the specified slave died, the Ila' falls because he becomes in a state where he can approach her without anything obligatory; and if he owns it by purchase or otherwise, the Ila' returns from the time of ownership if he had not had intercourse with her before; if he had intercourse before the new ownership, it does not return due to the fall of Ila'. And if the specified slave died before sale, the Ila' falls because of his ability to have intercourse without anything; and upon this detail is the death of a woman whose divorce was suspended or her being bain and then he marries her. And in al-Jami' li al-Sadr: he said 'you are divorces three times before I approach you by a month' or 'before I approach you' – it necessitates. His statement: (And it is restricted by the exception because if he said etc.) The wording of al-Walwalijiyya: a man said to his wife 'by Allah, I will not approach you for a year' – four months passed and she became bain, then he married her and another four months passed, she became bain again; then if he marries her a third time, it (the Ila') does not occur because less than four months remain of the year after marriage. His statement: (Contrary to 'every slave I own is free') i.e., where he becomes a muli according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf, because he cannot approach except with something obligatory upon him, and he cannot repel that by refraining because ownership may occur without his action.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the chapter of Ila' (oath of abstention from marital relations). The commentator, Imam Ibn Nujaym, expands on the rulings concerning which oaths make a husband a 'muli' (one who has taken an oath of Ila'). The core principle is that an oath becomes Ila' if it involves a burdensome act (yastashiqquhu) that the husband must perform if he approaches his wife, such as Hajj, fasting, charity, freeing a slave, or divorce. The passage details various cases and includes intra-Hanafi disputes, particularly with Abu Yusuf. The commentary begins by explaining the matn statement that if a man swears by Hajj, fasting, etc., or makes Ila' from a revocably divorced woman, he becomes a muli. It then clarifies that 'conceptual Ila'' (al-ila' al-ma'nawi) is an oath suspending an obligation upon approach. The commentator then discusses exceptions: if the husband says 'I will not approach you except for a day', he is not a muli because he can approach within four months on that day. Various scenarios are examined, such as when the wife is in a distant town, or when the oath is conditioned on events like killing or freeing a slave. The passage highlights that the burden must be real; acts like praying two rak'ahs are not considered burdensome (unless they are numerous like 100 rak'ahs), but acts like Hajj, sacrificing an animal, i'tikaf, and kaffarah are. A key dispute is about oaths involving unspecified slaves: if a husband says 'every slave I buy is free' upon approaching, he becomes a muli according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf. Similarly, an oath to free a specific slave makes him a muli, but if the slave dies or is sold, the Ila' may fall. The passage also discusses the effect of re-marrying after Ila' triggers divorce, and the details of when the Ila' returns. Overall, this is a detailed exposition of the legal nuances of Ila', emphasizing the Hanafi methodology of qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms
الإيلاء المعنوي (al-ila' al-ma'nawi): Conceptual Ila' where the oath is by suspending a burdensome act upon consummation, as opposed to the literal Ila' which is by Allah's name.
المولي (al-muli): The husband who takes an oath of Ila', i.e., swears to abstain from sexual relations with his wife.
يستشقه (yastashiqquhu): Something burdensome or hard on the self, used to determine whether an oath constitutes Ila'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the husband become a muli when he swears 'if I approach you, every slave I buy is free' according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) Because buying a slave is always burdensome.
B) Because ownership may occur without his choice, making the burden possible and unavoidable.
C) Because the oath is for more than four months.
D) Because freeing slaves is a religious duty.

Key Takeaway
An oath of Ila' is established by suspending a burdensome act upon approach, even if the burden is only possible; the relied-upon Hanafi position considers future slaves as such a burden.

The Criterion of Burden in Ila': Distinguishing Burdensome and Non-Burdensome Vows
Commentary expanding on the rulings of Ila' with examples and distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of mashaqqah to determine whether a conditional vow constitutes Ila' and distinguish between vows that make a husband a moli and those that do not.

Core Idea
A husband becomes a moli only if his conditional vow imposes a genuine hardship upon intercourse.

In the Hanafi school, the validity of Ila' depends on whether the vowed act is burdensome (mashaqqah). This lesson examines the detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, where the author distinguishes vows that cause hardship from those that are trivial. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly identifying when a husband is bound by the rules of Ila'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow and its condition
Read the husband's statement. Does he say 'If I have intercourse with you, then for Allah upon me is X'? This conditional structure is essential.
The vow must be explicitly conditional on intercourse.
2
Evaluate the burden of the vowed act
Determine whether the vowed act (e.g., Hajj, prayer, fasting) imposes a genuine hardship (mashaqqah) on the husband according to custom and legal consideration.
The commentary lists examples of burdensome and non-burdensome acts.
3
Apply the general rule and exceptions
If the act is burdensome (like Hajj, one hundred rak'ahs, sacrifice), the husband becomes a moli. If it is not burdensome (like two rak'ahs, following a funeral, tasbih), he does not.
Pay attention to the debate about whether prayer is always non-burdensome; the author concludes that the criterion is customary practice, not mere performance.
4
Consider special cases (emancipation, divorce)
Even if the vowed act is emancipation or divorce, the same principle applies. However, note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding conditional emancipation of unspecified slaves (Abu Yusuf's view).
The text includes rulings for 'every slave I buy is free' making one a moli.
5
Account for changes in the subject (selling, death)
If the subject of the vow (e.g., a specific slave) is sold or dies, the Ila' falls because the burden is removed. If it is reacquired, Ila' may return.
The passage details how selling a slave affects the Ila' period.

And included are the sacrifice, i'tikaf, oath, expiation of oath, and slaughter of a child, because he is obligated by nathr to slaughter a sheep according to us, as in al-Bada'i'. End quote. End quote. And if the specified slave is sold, the Ila' falls because he becomes in a state where he can have intercourse with her without anything incumbent upon him. And if he owns him by purchase or other, the Ila' returns from the time of ownership if he had not had sex with her before it. If he had sex with her before the renewed ownership, it does not return due to the falling of Ila'. And if the specified slave dies before sale, the Ila' falls due to his ability to have sex without anything. And upon this detail is the death of the woman whose divorce or irrevocable separation was suspended, then he marries her. And in al-Jami' li al-Sadr: He said 'You are divorced three times before I have sex with you by a month' or 'before I have sex with you', it is binding upon him his statement. And he said: (And he restricted by exception because if he said...) the expression of al-Walwalji: A man said to his wife 'By Allah I will not have sex with you for a year', then four months passed and she separated, then he married her, and four more months passed, she separated again. If he marries her a third time, it does not occur because there remained from the year after marriage less than four months. His statement: (Contrary to 'Every slave I own is free', meaning where he becomes a moli according to them, contrary to Abu Yusuf, because he cannot have intercourse except with something incumbent upon him, and he cannot avoid that by abandonment because ownership may be acquired without his action).

The duration of Ila' of a slave woman is two months. And if the moli is unable to have sex with her due to his illness or her illness or... and he becomes, and he becomes by a month if he has sex with her? Not a moli before the month and after it he becomes, except if he has sex in it, and the second is emphasis, contrary to 'he becomes', 'By Allah I will not have sex with you if I have sex with you' for suspension. He said 'You are divorced before I have sex with you' is executed. It is said: No, this is a moli. End quote. And in al-Khaniyya: He said to his wife 'If I have sex with you, my slave is free', then four months passed and she sued him to the judge and he separated them, then the slave presented evidence that he was originally free. The judge rules for his freedom and nullifies the Ila' and returns the woman to her husband because it became clear that he was not a moli. End quote. As for the validity of Ila' from a revocably divorced woman, even if she has no right to intercourse, it is considered because her intercourse is permissible. If she observes her 'iddah by menstrual cycles, due to the possibility that her 'iddah may extend until the Ila' period passes and she separates. And if by months, due to the possibility that he may take her back before it passes. If he does not take her back until her 'iddah passes before the Ila' period passes, the Ila' falls due to its subject being lost from the divorced woman. For its contract in the matter of the obligation of expiation upon violation, because the contract of an oath depends on sensory conception not legal; do you not see that it is contracted upon what is disobedience? And in al-Khaniyya: A man made Ila' from his wife, then divorced her with an irrevocable divorce. If four months pass from the time of Ila' while she is in her 'iddah, another divorce occurs by Ila'. If her 'iddah ends and then the Ila' period completes, divorce falls by Ila'. A man made Ila' from his wife, then divorced her, then married her. If he married her before the expiration of the 'iddah, the Ila' remains as it was, so if four months complete from the time of Ila', another divorce occurs by the rule of Ila'. If he married her after divorcing her after... al-Farsi with inheritance and he cannot refuse it. 'You are divorced if I have sex with you', so he is a moli. Thus in the commentary of his statement: (Then he bought it and sold the other or preceded its sale, I did not find his statement 'or preceded its sale' in Talkhis al-Khilati nor in its commentary; perhaps it is an expression of Talkhis al-Shahid. Al-Farsi said: A man said to his wife 'If I have sex with you, my two slaves are free', he becomes a moli. If he sold one, the Ila' falls in his regard because if he were alone and sold it, the Ila' falls; similarly here, and the Ila' remains in regard to the one he did not sell because it remains a locus for emancipation. If he bought the one he sold then sold the other, the first period is void and the period begins from the time of purchase. And this is because the moli is one who cannot have intercourse except with one thing incumbent from the beginning of the period to its end. And if the existence of the preventing factor is something that changes, he is only a moli from the time of purchase because the condition was absent before; before the sale, intercourse would necessitate freeing both, after it freeing one (the remaining), and after the purchase freeing the purchased one. The preventing factor becomes unified in the entire period from the time of purchase. And in the case where he said 'One of these two slaves is free', the issue remaining as it was, he becomes a moli from the time he swore because the preventing factor, which is freeing one, did not change because it existed from the beginning of the period.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the chapter of Ila' (oath of abstinence) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author explains the rulings that determine when an oath makes a husband a 'moli' (one who has taken an oath of abstinence, triggering a legal process). The core principle is that Ila' is established only if the husband's conditional vow imposes a true hardship upon him when he has intercourse with his wife. If the vowed act is something easy or not burdensome, the oath is not considered Ila'. The author begins by listing vows that are burdensome, such as vowing to perform Hajj, which makes the husband a moli. Then he excludes vows that are not burdensome, like vowing to pray two rak'ahs (even in Bayt al-Maqdis) because the specific location does not increase the obligation. Similarly, following a funeral, performing a prostration of recitation, reciting Quran, or saying tasbih are not burdensome. However, if the husband vows one hundred rak'ahs, that is burdensome and constitutes Ila'. The commentator refutes the previous commentator's blanket statement that prayer is not burdensome, explaining that the reasoning should be that prayer is not customarily sworn by, so even a small prayer is like funeral prayer. The passage then includes other vows that make one a moli: offering a sacrifice, i'tikaf, taking an oath, expiation of an oath, and slaughter of a child (as a nazr), because fulfilling these imposes financial or physical hardship. Regarding fasting, the ruling depends on whether the fast is specified. If unspecified (e.g., 'a day' or 'a month'), it is Ila' because the duration could extend beyond the Ila' period? Actually, the text says unspecified fast is Ila' because the husband could delay intercourse until he fulfills the fast, but if the fast is specified for less than the Ila' period (e.g., 'this month'), he can just wait and then have intercourse without any obligation, so it is not Ila'. If specified for the Ila' period or more (e.g., 'four months'), it is Ila'. For emancipation and divorce, the ruling applies broadly: whether the slave or wife is specific or unspecified, and whether the vow is immediate or conditional. For example, saying 'Every slave I buy is free' makes him a moli (contrary to Abu Yusuf), and 'Every woman I marry is divorced' also makes him a moli. The author then discusses cases where the subject of the vow changes (selling, buying, death of a slave or wife). If the specified slave is sold, the Ila' falls because the husband can now have intercourse without incurring any obligation. But if he later reacquires the slave (by purchase or other means), the Ila' returns from the time of acquisition unless he had intercourse in the interim. If the slave dies, Ila' also falls. The same applies to a wife whose divorce or separation was suspended: if she dies or becomes permanently separated, the Ila' falls. The passage includes a quote from al-Walwalji about a year-long oath: if the wife separates after four months, then he remarries her, a new four-month period starts, and if he remarries her a third time with less than four months remaining, Ila' does not occur. The author clarifies that in the case of 'every slave I own is free', the husband becomes a moli because ownership might be acquired without his choice, so he cannot avoid the obligation. On page 111, the duration of Ila' for a slave woman is two months (half of the free woman's four months). The passage also discusses situations where the moli is unable to have intercourse due to illness (his or hers). There is a subtle discussion about conditional oaths: if the husband says 'If I have sex with you, my slave is free' and the slave later proves he was originally free, the Ila' is nullified and the marriage continues. Then the author discusses Ila' from a revocably divorced woman: it is valid because intercourse remains permissible, and the 'iddah might coincide with the Ila' period. From an irrevocably divorced woman or an ajnabiyya (stranger), Ila' is invalid because there is no marital relationship. However, if the husband swears an oath regarding such a woman and violates it, he must still pay expiation because the contract of an oath depends on physical possibility, not legal permissibility. The passage ends with a discussion of delayed penalty (ta'khir al-jaza') and a dispute about whether selling a slave in a two-slave oath resets the Ila' period. The relied-upon position (tarjih) is from al-Farsi: if one slave is sold, the Ila' falls for that slave but continues for the other; if the first is repurchased and then the second sold, the period starts anew from the purchase.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): An oath taken by a husband to abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife for a period (usually four months), which, if not broken, can lead to divorce.
مول (mūlī): The husband who has taken an oath of Ila'.
فيء (fay'): The act of the husband returning to sexual relations with his wife, thus breaking the oath and becoming liable for the penalty.
مشقة (mashaqqah): Hardship or difficulty; a criterion for determining whether a vowed act makes one a moli.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following conditional vows would make the husband a moli?
A) If I have intercourse with you, for Allah upon me is two rak'ahs of prayer.
B) If I have intercourse with you, for Allah upon me is one hundred rak'ahs.
C) If I have intercourse with you, for Allah upon me is following a funeral.
D) If I have intercourse with you, for Allah upon me is a tasbih.

Key Takeaway
A conditional vow makes a husband a moli only if the vowed act is burdensome (mashaqqah) and the condition is unavoidable; this principle determines the application of Ila' rulings.
