Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Duration of Ila' for a Slave Woman and Effects of Emancipation
Ruling with proof on Ila' duration for slave women and emancipation effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the duration of Ila' for a slave woman in the Hanafi school and explain how emancipation during the Ila' period affects the duration.

Core Idea
The Ila' period for a slave woman is two months because slavery halves the four-month duration for free women.

In the Hanafi school, the duration of Ila'—an oath to abstain from marital relations—is not uniform for all wives. This passage specifies that for a slave woman, the period is two months, due to the principle that slavery halves religious obligations. It also addresses the complex scenario where a slave wife is emancipated during the Ila' period, changing the duration to that of a free woman. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying Ila' in cases involving slave wives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is Ila' made by a husband against his slave wife. The ruling: the duration is two months, not four.
The principle 'al-raqq munassif' (slavery halves) is the basis.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is qiyas: the four-month period for free women is halved because slavery reduces obligations by half.
This is a derived rule, not directly from Quran or Sunnah.
3
Apply the case of emancipation during Ila'
If the slave woman is emancipated during the Ila' period, the period becomes four months from the date of emancipation.
This applies whether the husband divorced her revocably or irrevocably before emancipation.
4
Note the exception: Ila' from one's own slave girl
Ila' is invalid if the woman is a slave owned by the husband, because the condition (mahalliyya) requires a wife.
The passage clarifies this objection.
5
Work through the fur' from al-Jami' al-Kabir
For a man with a free wife and a slave wife who swears not to approach either: the slave wife's period ends first; if she becomes free before her period ends, it extends to four months.
This illustrates the interplay of multiple periods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأجنبية لا ومدة ايلاء الأمة شهران لأن الرق منصف . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الزوج حراً أو عبداً ذكره الاسبيجابي . يرد عليه الإيلاء من أمته لأن شرطه المحلية وهي بالزوجية كما قدمناه، ولو طلقها زوجها بعد الإيلاء رجعياً أو بائناً ثم أعتقت في المدة انتقلت المدة إلى مدة إيلاء الحرائر ذكره الاسبيجابي. وفي الجامع الكبير للصدر الشهيد تحته حرة وأمة حلف لا يقرب إحداهما ومضي شهران بانت الأمة لسبق مدتها، فلو عتقت قبلها كملت مدتها، وكذا لو أبانها ثم عتقت بخلاف العدة فلو مضت مدة أخرى بانت الحرة.
And the non-married woman (does not count), and the duration of Ila' of a slave woman is two months because slavery halves. This is absolute, so it includes whether the husband is free or a slave, as mentioned by al-Isbijabi. It is objected that Ila' from his slave girl is not valid because its condition is marital capacity (mahalliyya), which is by marriage as we have stated. And if her husband divorces her after Ila' revocably or irrevocably, then she is emancipated during the period, the period transfers to the period of Ila' of free women, mentioned by al-Isbijabi. And in al-Jami' al-Kabir of al-Sadr al-Shahid: Under him a free woman and a slave woman, he swore not to approach either, then two months passed; the slave woman is separated by Ila' because her period precedes. So if she was emancipated before it, her period is completed. Similarly, if he irrevocably divorced her then she was emancipated, unlike the waiting period. So if another period passed, the free woman is separated.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the duration of Ila' for a slave woman and the effect of emancipation. The author states that the period of Ila' for a slave woman is two months, because slavery reduces obligations by half. This applies regardless of whether the husband is free or a slave. However, Ila' from one's own slave girl is invalid because the condition for Ila' is that the woman be a wife; a slave girl owned by the husband is not a wife. If a husband makes Ila' of his wife and then divorces her (revocably or irrevocably) and she is emancipated during the Ila' period, the period changes to that of a free woman (four months) because she is now free. This is mentioned by al-Isbijabi. The author then quotes al-Jami' al-Kabir: If a man has both a free wife and a slave wife and swears not to approach either, then two months pass, the slave wife is separated by Ila' because her shorter period ends first. If she becomes free before those two months, her period is completed as a free woman (four months). Similarly, if he irrevocably divorces her and then she is emancipated, the period changes. This is unlike the waiting period ('idda) which does not change upon emancipation after irrevocable divorce. Then if another four-month period passes, the free wife is also separated.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-ila'): An oath by a husband not to approach his wife for a period of four months (or two for slaves).
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave who is married.
الرق منصف (al-raqq munassif): Slavery halves religious obligations and punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) One month

Key Takeaway
The Ila' period for a slave woman is two months, halved from the free woman's four months due to the principle that slavery halves religious obligations.

Fi'yah (Return) When Husband Unable to Consummate
Ruling on fi'yah when husband unable to consummate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on fi'yah when the husband is unable to consummate and identify the conditions under which verbal fi'yah is valid.

Core Idea
When consummation is impossible, verbal fi'yah suffices — the husband's return is by word, not deed.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on how a husband who has made an oath of Ila' but is physically unable to consummate makes his return (fi'yah). The key question: does the obligation of fi'yah always require actual intercourse? The text clarifies that when genuine inability exists, a verbal declaration of return is sufficient, because the husband's harm was by word and can be appeased by word.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on fi'yah when unable
Identify the act (fi'yah) and its ruling (wajib). When the husband cannot consummate, he must make fi'yah by saying 'I return to you' or similar.
2
Identify the underlying reasoning (talil)
The husband harmed her by mentioning the prohibition (the Ila'), so appeasing her with a verbal promise suffices.
This is a rational proof (istihsan).
3
List the causes of inability that allow verbal fi'yah
Include his illness, her illness, atresia, minority, distance, wife's refusal or nushuz, separation by judge, imprisonment.
These scenarios prevent actual intercourse.
4
Distinguish when verbal fi'yah does NOT suffice
If the husband can consummate (e.g., distance is traversable, prison allows intercourse), actual intercourse is required.
Verbal fi'yah is only for genuine inability.
5
Recognize acceptable verbal formulas
Any phrase indicating return (e.g., 'I take you back', 'I revoke the Ila') is valid, not just 'fi'tu ilayha'.
The key is clear indication of returning to normal relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عجز المولى عن وطئها بمرضه أو مرضها أو بالرتق أو بالصغر أو بعد مسافة ففيؤه إن يقول فئت إليها لأنه أذاها بذكر المنع فيكون إرضاؤها بالوعد باللسان. أراد ببعد المسافة أن يكون منهما مسافة لا يقدر على قطعها في مدة الإيلاء، فإن قدر لا يصح فيؤه باللسان كما في البدائع وقيد بالقول لأن المريض لو فاء بقلبه لا بلسانه لا يعتبر؛ كذا الخانية. وليس مراده خوص لفظ فئت إليها بل ما يدل عليه كقوله رجعتك أو راجعتك أو ارتجعتك أو أبطلت الإيلاء أو رجعت عما قلت ونحوه ودخل تحت العجز أن تكون ممتنعة منه أو كانت في مكان لا يعرفه وهي ناشزة أو حال القاضي بينهما لشهادة الطلاق الثلاث للتزكية أو كانت محبوسة أو محبوساً إذا لم يقدر على مجامعتها في السجن فإن قدر عليه ففيؤه إلى آخرها.
And if the husband is unable to have intercourse with her due to his illness, or her illness, or due to atresia, or minority, or distance, then his fi'yah is that he says 'I return to you', because he harmed her by mentioning the prohibition, so appeasing her is by promise with the tongue. He intended by 'distance' that there be a distance between them that he cannot traverse within the Ila' period; if he can, his fi'yah by speech is not valid as in al-Bada'i'. And it is restricted to speech because if a patient returns (fi'a) with his heart but not his tongue, it is not considered; as in al-Khaniyya. And his intention is not the specific wording 'fi'tu ilayha' but rather what indicates it, like saying 'I returned you' or 'I take you back' or 'I revoke the Ila' and similar. And included under inability are: that she refuses him, or she is in a place he does not know and she is disobedient (nashiz), or the judge separated between them due to testimony of triple talaq for verification, or she is imprisoned or he is imprisoned if he cannot have intercourse with her in prison; if he can, his fi'yah is to her.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on fi'yah (return) for a husband who has made Ila' but is physically unable to consummate the marriage during the Ila' period. The Hanafi school holds that in such cases, the husband can make fi'yah verbally by saying 'I return to you' or similar phrases. The reason is that he caused her harm by vowing abstinence, so it suffices to appease her with a verbal promise. The inability can be due to his illness, her illness, atresia (a physical obstruction), her young age, or if they are at a distance that cannot be traveled within the Ila' period. If the distance is traversable, then fi'yah by speech is not valid; he must actually consummate. Fi'yah is only by speech, not by intention alone. The specific wording is not required; any clear indication of returning to normal marital relations suffices, such as 'I take you back' or 'I revoke the Ila'. 'Inability' also includes situations where the wife is rebellious (nashiz), in an unknown location, or if a judge has separated them due to doubts about a triple divorce, or if either is imprisoned and cannot have intercourse. However, if they can have intercourse in prison, fi'yah must be actual intercourse.

Key Terms
الفئ (al-fi'yah): The husband's return to normal marital relations after an oath of Ila', either by intercourse or verbal declaration when unable.
الرتق (al-ratq): A medical condition in women causing an obstruction preventing intercourse.
الناشزة (al-nashizah): A wife who is disobedient and refuses intimacy without a valid reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what suffices for fi'yah when the husband is unable to have intercourse due to illness?
A) He must send a message
B) He must say 'I return to you' or similar
C) He must wait until he recovers and then have intercourse
D) He must pay compensation

Key Takeaway
Verbal fi'yah is valid when the husband cannot consummate due to legitimate reasons; the key is that the remedy matches the harm.

The Period of Ila' for a Slave Wife and Related Rulings
Rulings on the period of ila' for a slave wife and related cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the period of ila' for a slave wife, apply the rule when she is emancipated during the period, and distinguish the Hanafi position from Zufar's on conditional manumission.

Core Idea
Ila' for a slave wife lasts two months; emancipation may extend it to four months if the husband is free.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the duration of ila' when the wife is an enslaved woman. The key ruling is that slavery halves the period to two months. However, various scenarios—such as emancipation during the period, multiple wives, and divorce—modify this ruling. The passage also addresses a related dispute with Zufar about conditional manumission. We will break down these rules step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base period for ila' of a slave wife
Find the statement that explicitly gives the period. Note that it applies regardless of whether the husband is free or a slave.
The text says 'wa muddatu ila'i al-amati shahrani' (the period is two months).
2
Understand the effect of emancipation during the ila' period
Read the sentence about emancipation after revocable or irrevocable divorce. Determine how the period changes based on the husband's status.
Al-Isbijabi states that if the wife is emancipated, the period transfers to that of free women (four months) only if the husband is free.
3
Analyze the scenario with a free and a slave wife
Follow the example from al-Jami' al-Kabir: a man swears not to approach either of his two wives (one free, one slave). After two months, the slave wife becomes divorced. Consider what happens if she is emancipated before the two months or after an irrevocable divorce.
The slave wife divorces after two months; the free wife after another period. Emancipation before two months completes her period (still two months).
4
Contrast Abu Yusuf's view on specification
In the two-wife scenario, Abu Yusuf holds that the slave wife becomes specified (like breaking the oath) and if he remarries her after irrevocable divorce, the ila' returns. Compare this with the standard view.
The text says 'wa an Abi Yusuf la wa tata'ayyanu lahu al-amah' (Abu Yusuf says no, the slave wife is specified for him).
5
Examine the dispute with Zufar on conditional manumission
The passage ends with a ruling on the statement 'If I buy a slave girl, she is free.' The Hanafi position limits this to slaves already in his possession. Zufar extends it to future purchases. Understand the reasoning.
The Hanafi view: the condition is future and uncertain, so it only applies to current slaves. Zufar: it is a valid oath-like suspension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومدة إيلاء الأمة شهران لأن الرق منصف . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الزوج حراً أو عبداً ذكره الاسبيجابي . يرد عليه الإيلاء من أمته لأن شرطه المحلية وهي بالزوجية كما قدمناه، ولو طلقها زوجها بعد الإيلاء رجعياً أو بائناً ثم أعتقت في المدة انتقلت المدة إلى مدة إيلاء الحرائر ذكره الاسبيجابي. وفي الجامع الكبير للصدر الشهيد تحته حرة وأمة حلف لا يقرب إحداهما ومضي شهران بانت الأمة لسبق مدتها، فلو عتقت قبلها كملت مدتها، وكذا لو أبانها ثم عتقت بخلاف العدة فلو مضت مدة أخرى بانت الحرة. وعن أبي يوسف لا وتتعين له الأمة كالحنث فإن تزوجها بعد البينونة عاد إيلاؤها وكذا هما لكن إن رتب بانت الأولى عند تمام مدتها من وقت العقد والثانية بمدة ثانية بخلاف ما لو بانت قبلها قال لامرأته وأمته والله لا أقرب إحداكما لم يكن مولياً، وكذا لو أعتق الأمة ثم تزوجها ومن وطئها كفر ويمكنه تركه كالأجنبية بخلاف واحدة منكما لعمومه وعلى هذا لو قال لزوجته لا أقرب إحداكما أو واحدة منكما لعمومه استحساناً. قال إن قربت إحداكما فالأخرى علي كظهر أمي وبانت إحداهما بالإيلاء أو بغيره بطل إيلاء الأخرى بخلاف فالأخرى طالق ما دامت في العدة، ولو قال فإحداكما أو فواحدة أو فهي لا لتعينها قال إن اشتريت جارية فهي حرة صح فيمن في ملكه دون من يملكها خلافاً لزفر .
And the period of ila' of a slave woman is two months, because slavery halves [the period]. He made it absolute, so it includes whether the husband is a free man or a slave, mentioned by al-Isbijabi. It is objected: ila' from his own slave woman [is valid?] because its condition is conjugality, which is by marriage as we have presented. And if her husband divorces her after ila' revocably or irrevocably, then she is emancipated during the period, the period transfers to the period of ila' of free women, mentioned by al-Isbijabi. And in al-Jami' al-Kabir by al-Sadr al-Shahid: Under him there is a free woman and a slave woman; he swore not to approach either of them; two months pass, the slave woman becomes irrevocably divorced due to the earlier end of her period. If she is emancipated before it, her period is completed. Likewise if he divorces her irrevocably then she is emancipated, unlike the waiting period. Then if another period passes, the free woman becomes irrevocably divorced. And from Abu Yusuf: no, and the slave woman is specified for him like the oath. If he marries her after irrevocable divorce, his ila' towards her returns, and likewise both, but if he arranged it, the first becomes irrevocably divorced at the completion of her period from the time of contract, and the second after a second period, unlike if she became irrevocably divorced before that. He said to his wife and his slave woman: By Allah, I will not approach one of you; he is not a muli. Likewise if he emancipates the slave woman then marries her; and whoever has intercourse with her disbelieves? and he can abandon her like a foreign woman, unlike 'one of you two' due to its generality. And on this, if he said to his wife: I will not approach one of you two, or one of you two, due to its generality by istihsan. He said: If I approach one of you two, then the other is like my mother's back, and one of them becomes irrevocably divorced by ila' or otherwise, the ila' of the other is invalidated, unlike 'the other is divorced' as long as she is in the waiting period. And if he said: 'one of you' or 'or one' or 'or she' not for specification? He said: If I buy a slave girl, she is free; it is valid for those in his possession, not for those he does not possess, contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the duration of ila' when the wife is an enslaved woman. The base ruling states that the period for a slave wife is two months because slavery halves the rights and obligations. This applies whether the husband is free or a slave. If the wife is emancipated during the ila' period, the period extends to that of a free woman (four months) if the husband is free, as per al-Isbijabi. The text then examines a scenario from al-Jami' al-Kabir where a man has both a free and a slave wife and swears not to approach either. After two months, the slave wife becomes irrevocably divorced because her period ends first. If she is emancipated before the two months, her period is completed (i.e., she still divorces after two months from the oath, not four). If the husband divorces her irrevocably and she is then emancipated, the period remains two months, unlike the case of revocable divorce where the waiting period changes. Abu Yusuf holds that in the scenario with two wives, the slave wife becomes specified for him like breaking the oath—if he marries her after irrevocable divorce, his ila' returns. The text also covers the ruling when a man says to his wife and slave woman, 'I will not approach one of you'—he is not considered a muli because the oath includes a non-wife. Similarly, if he later marries the slave woman after emancipating her, the oath does not make him a muli. The passage ends with a dispute with Zufar regarding the validity of a conditional manumission on purchase of a slave girl.

Key Terms
إيلاء (ila'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife, leading to automatic divorce after a specified period
الأمة (al-amah): An enslaved woman who is legally recognized as a concubine or slave
الطلاق البائن (al-talaq al-ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately and cannot be revoked without a new contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The period remains two months from the start.
B) The period extends to four months from the start.
C) The ila' becomes void.
D) The husband must renew the oath.

Key Takeaway
The period of ila' for a slave wife is two months, but emancipation during the period extends it to four months if the husband is free; additionally, a conditional manumission on future purchase is restricted to current slaves in the Hanafi school.

Conditions for Valid Fi' by Speech in Ila'
Conditions for fi' bi al-lisan and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid fi' by speech and distinguish real inability from legal inability.

Core Idea
Fi' by speech is only valid when genuine inability prevents intercourse, not when the husband is legally barred by his own choice.

When a husband takes a vow to abstain from intercourse (ila') and then is genuinely unable to consummate due to illness, distance, or similar excuses, the Hanafi school allows him to 'return' (fi') by verbal declaration alone. This lesson unpacks the specific conditions that must be met for that verbal return to be legally effective, and reveals an important nuance: not all forms of inability qualify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is the husband's verbal declaration of return (fi' bi al-lisan) when he is unable to have intercourse. The ruling is that it is a valid substitute for actual intercourse under specific conditions.
The default ruling is that fi' requires intercourse; speech is an exception.
2
List the conditions for validity
From the passage, identify the three core conditions: (1) real inability (illness, distance, etc.), (2) inability persists throughout the ila' period, and (3) marriage is still valid at the time of verbal fi'.
Real inability excludes legal inability like ihram.
3
Distinguish real versus legal inability
The passage explicitly distinguishes 'real inability' (e.g., illness, imprisonment) from 'legal inability' (e.g., ihram for Hajj with enough time). Legal inability does not permit fi' by speech because the husband caused it by his own choice.
This distinction is crucial for many exam questions.
4
Understand the effect on the oath
Fi' by speech nullifies the automatic divorce effect of ila' but does not dissolve the original oath. If the husband later has intercourse, he must pay kaffarah for breaking the oath.
This is why the passage says fi' by speech is effective 'in respect of divorce' but not 'in respect of breaking the oath'.
5
Apply the condition of valid marriage
The final condition: at the moment of verbal fi', the wife must not be irrevocably divorced. If she is, verbal fi' is ineffective; only actual intercourse can then break the ila'.
This shows the importance of timing in fi'.

قوله : (وإن عجز المولى عن وطئها بمرضه أو مرضها أو بالرتق أو بالصغر أو بعد مسافة ففيؤه إن يقول فئت إليها لأنه أذاها بذكر المنع فيكون إرضاؤها بالوعد باللسان. أراد ببعد المسافة أن يكون منهما مسافة لا يقدر على قطعها في مدة الإيلاء، فإن قدر لا يصح فيؤه باللسان كما في البدائع وقيد بالقول لأن المريض لو فاء بقلبه لا بلسانه لا يعتبر ؛ كذا الخانية. وليس مراده خوص لفظ فئت إليها بل ما يدل عليه كقوله رجعتك أو راجعتك أو ارتجعتك أو أبطلت الإيلاء أو رجعت عما قلت ونحوه ودخل تحت العجز أن تكون ممتنعة منه أو كانت في مكان لا يعرفه وهي ناشزة أو حال القاضي بينهما لشهادة الطلاق الثلاث للتزكية أو كانت محبوسة أو محبوساً إذا لم يقدر على مجامعتها في السجن فإن قدر عليه ففيؤه إلى آخرها . اهـ ملخصا قوله : (بخلاف العدة فإنها إذا طلقت طلاقاً بائناً ثم أعتقت لا تنقلب عدتها عدة الحرائر وفي الطلاق الرجعي تنقلب؛ كذا في البدائع . قوله : (قال إن اشتريت جارية فهي حرة الخ) كذا في النسخ ولعلها تحريف والأصل إن تسريت قوله: (أو محبوساً) هذا على ما في شرح مختصر الكرخي للقدوري. قال في الفتح : وصححه في البدائع . قلت : وعبارة البدائع بعد نقل ما في شرح المختصر : وذكر القاضي في شرحه مختصر الجماع؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وقيد بما ذكره من أنواع العجز الحقيقي احترازاً عن العجز الحكمي مثل أن يكون محرماً وقت الإيلاء وبينه وبينه الحج أربعة أشهر، فعندنا لا يكون فيؤه إلا بالجماع لأن المتسبب باختياره بطريق محظور فيما لزمه فلا يستحق تخفيفاً. وأراد بكون الفيء باللسان معتبراً مبطلاً للإيلاء في حق الطلاق، أما في حق بقاء اليمين باعتبار الحنث فلا، حتى لو وطئها بعد الفيء باللسان في مدة الإيلاء لزمته الكفارة لتحقق الحنث. وفي البدائع : ومن شروط صحة الفيء بالقول قيام ملك النكاح وقت الفيء بالقول وهو أن يكون في حال ما يفئ إليها زوجته غير بائنة منه، فإن كانت بائنة منه ففاء بلسانه لم يكن ذلك فيئاً ويبقى الإيلاء لأن الفيء بالقول حال قيام النكاح إنما يرفع الإيلاء في حق حكم الطلاق بحصول إيفاء حقها به ولا حق لها حالة البينونة بخلاف الفيء بالجماع فإنه يصح بعد ثبوت البينونة حتى لا يبقى الإيلاء بل يبطل لأنه حنث بالوطء فانحلت اليمين وبطلت ولم يوجد الحنث ها هنا فلا تنحل اليمين فلا يرتفع الإيلاء اهـ
His statement: 'And if the muli is unable to have intercourse with her due to his illness or her illness, or due to atresia, or minority, or distance, then his fi’ is that he says ‘I have returned to her,’ because he harmed her by mentioning the prohibition, so satisfying her is by verbal promise. He intended by ‘distance’ that there be a distance between them that he cannot cover within the period of ila’. If he is able, his fi’ by speech is not valid, as in al-Bada’i‘. And he restricted it to speech because if the patient returns with his heart but not his tongue, it is not considered; so in al-Khaniyya. And he does not mean the specific wording ‘I have returned to her,’ but whatever indicates it, like his saying ‘I take you back,’ or ‘I return you,’ or ‘I revoke the ila’,’ or ‘I retract what I said,’ and the like. And under ‘inability’ comes: if she is preventing herself from him, or she is in a place he does not know while she is disobedient, or the judge separates them due to the testimony of triple divorce for tazkiya, or she is imprisoned, or he is imprisoned if he cannot have intercourse with her in prison; if he can, then his fi’ is to the end of it. End summary. His statement: ‘Contrary to the waiting period, for if she is divorced irrevocably then emancipated, her waiting period does not become that of free women, while in revocable divorce it becomes; so in al-Bada’i‘.’ His statement: ‘He said: If I buy a slave girl, she is free, etc.’ The text has it like this, perhaps it is a corruption; the original is ‘If I take a concubine’. His statement: ‘or imprisoned’ is according to what is in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi by al-Quduri. He said in al-Fath: ‘And he corrected it in al-Bada’i‘.’ I said: ‘The wording of al-Bada’i‘ after quoting from Sharh al-Mukhtasar: And the judge mentioned in his Sharh Mukhtasar al-Jami‘; so in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he restricted it to the types of real inability to avoid legal inability, such as being in ihram at the time of ila’ and between him and him four months of hajj; then according to us, his fi’ is only by intercourse because he caused it by his choice through a forbidden means regarding what he must fulfill, so he does not deserve leniency. And he intended that the fi’ by speech is considered effective in nullifying the ila’ regarding the ruling of divorce; as for the survival of the oath in terms of breaking it, no. So if he has intercourse with her after fi’ by speech during the period of ila’, the expiation becomes necessary due to the occurrence of breaking the oath. And in al-Bada’i‘: Among the conditions for validity of fi’ by speech is the existence of the marriage bond at the time of fi’ by speech, meaning that at the time he returns to her she is his wife not irrevocably divorced. If she is irrevocably divorced, his fi’ by speech is not fi’ and the ila’ remains, because fi’ by speech during the existence of marriage only removes the ila’ regarding the ruling of divorce due to fulfillment of her right, while she has no right during irrevocable divorce, unlike fi’ by intercourse, which is valid after irrevocable divorce, such that the ila’ does not remain but is invalidated, because he broke the oath by intercourse, so the oath is dissolved and invalidated. And here that breaking did not occur, so the oath is not dissolved, so the ila’ is not removed. End.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the conditions under which a muli (husband who has taken a vow of abstinence) can perform fi’ (return) by words alone rather than by actual intercourse. The basic principle is that if the husband is genuinely incapable of consummation due to specific real excuses (illness of either spouse, atresia, minority, or a distance that cannot be covered within the ila’ period), then his verbal declaration of return is sufficient and valid. The author lists various forms of verbal return, not limited to the word 'fi’t' but any expression indicating his intention to resume marital relations. The text also distinguishes real inability from legal inability: if the husband is in a state of ihram during Hajj and has four months before the pilgrimage, he is considered legally unable due to his own choice, so fi’ by speech is not accepted; he must actually have intercourse. The passage further clarifies that fi’ by speech nullifies the ila’ only with respect to the automatic divorce; it does not dissolve the original oath. If the husband later has intercourse, he must still pay kaffarah for breaking his oath. A key condition from al-Bada’i‘ is that the marriage must still be valid at the time of the verbal fi’: if the wife has become irrevocably divorced, verbal return does not work—only actual intercourse can then break the ila’. This commentary thus systematically delineates the exceptions and conditions for fi’ by speech to prevent misuse.

Key Terms
الفيء باللسان (al-fay' bi al-lisan): The husband's verbal declaration of returning to his wife as a substitute for actual intercourse in ila’ when he is unable
العجز الحقيقي (al-'ajz al-haqiqi): Actual physical or practical inability to have intercourse, such as illness or distance
العجز الحكمي (al-'ajz al-hukmi): Legal or constructive inability, such as being in ihram or under a travel restriction that is self-imposed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for valid fi' by speech according to the passage?
A) The husband must say a specific Arabic phrase.
B) The husband must be genuinely unable to have intercourse.
C) The marriage must be valid at the time of fi'.
D) The inability must exist for the entire period.

Key Takeaway
Fi' by speech is a valid substitute for intercourse only when the husband is genuinely unable to consummate, the marriage is valid, and the inability persists throughout the ila' period; it nullifies the divorce effect but not the oath.

Conditions for Valid al-Fay' by Speech in Ila'
Explains when verbal fay' is valid vs. requires intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid fay' by speech in ila' and apply them to scenarios of real vs. legal incapacity.

Core Idea
Fay' by speech is valid only if the husband is continuously unable to have intercourse and the marriage remains intact.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains when a husband's verbal return (fay' bil-lisan) in ila' is acceptable. It distinguishes real incapacity from legal incapacity and sets strict conditions. We will unpack the three conditions and the important qiyas to tayammum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction: real vs. legal incapacity
The text contrasts real physical inability (e.g., illness) with legal inability (e.g., ihram). Only real incapacity allows fay' by speech.
Legal incapacity due to the husband's own choice (e.g., ihram) does not qualify.
2
State the three conditions for fay' by speech
From the summary: (1) incapacity at the time of intercourse, (2) continuous incapacity from ila' to end of period, (3) marriage bond still intact at time of fay'.
These are the shurut for validity.
3
Analyze the qiyas with tayammum
If the husband becomes able to have intercourse during the period, fay' by speech becomes invalid, just as tayammum is invalidated if water is found during prayer.
The illa is ability to perform the original before the substitute achieves its purpose.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
In perpetual ila' with illness and recovery, Abu Yusuf allows fay' by speech after recovery and remarriage, while the majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) do not.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

الجماع؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وقيد بما ذكره من أنواع العجز الحقيقي احترازاً عن العجز الحكمي مثل أن يكون محرماً وقت الإيلاء وبينه وبين الحج أربعة أشهر، فعندنا لا يكون فيؤه إلا بالجماع لأن المتسبب باختياره بطريق محظور فيما لزمه فلا يستحق تخفيفاً. وأراد بكون الفيء باللسان معتبراً مبطلاً للإيلاء في حق الطلاق، أما في حق بقاء اليمين باعتبار الحنث فلا، حتى لو وطئها بعد الفيء باللسان في مدة الإيلاء لزمته الكفارة لتحقق الحنث. وفي البدائع : ومن شروط صحة الفيء بالقول قيام ملك النكاح وقت الفيء بالقول وهو أن يكون في حال ما يفئ إليها زوجته غير بائنة منه، فإن كانت بائنة منه ففاء بلسانه لم يكن ذلك فيئاً ويبقى الإيلاء لأن الفيء بالقول حال قيام النكاح إنما يرفع الإيلاء في حق حكم الطلاق بحصول إيفاء حقها به ولا حق لها حالة البينونة بخلاف الفيء بالجماع فإنه يصح بعد ثبوت البينونة حتى لا يبقى الإيلاء بل يبطل لأنه حنث بالوطء فانحلت اليمين وبطلت ولم يوجد الحنث ها هنا فلا تنحل اليمين فلا يرتفع الإيلاء اهـ قوله : (وإن قدر في المدة ففيؤه الوطء) لكونه خلفاً عنه فإذا قدر على الأصل قبل حصول المقصود بالبدل بطل كالمتيمم إذا رأى الماء في صلاته. قيد بكونه في المدة لأنه لو قدر عليه بعدها لا يبطل، وشمل كلامه ما إذا كان قادراً وقت الإيلاء ثم عجز بشرط أن يمضي زمان يقدر على وطئها بعد الإيلاء، وما إذا كان عاجزاً وقته ثم قدر في المدة، وأما لو آلى إيلاء مؤبداً وهو مريض فبانت بمضي المدة ثم صح وتزوجها وهو مريض ففاء بلسانه لم يصح عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف وصححوا قوله؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي الجامع الكبير للصدر : الجامع أصل واللسان خلفه آلى في مرضه وفاء بلسانه بطل إيلاؤه في حق الطلاق فإن صح قبل تمام المدة بطل لقدرته على الأصل كالمتيمم، ولو لم يفيء حتى بانت فصح ثم مرض فتزوجها ففيؤه بالجماع وعن أبي يوسف وزفر لأنه الطحاوي أنه لو آلى من امرأته وهي محبوسة أو هو محبوس أو كان بينه وبين امرأته أقل من أربعة أشهر إلا أن العدو أو السلطان منعه عن ذلك فإن فيئه لا يكون إلا بالفعل ويمكن أن يوفق بين القولين في الحبس بأن يحمل ما ذكره القاضي على أن يقدر أحدهما على أن يصل إلى صاحبه في السجن والوجه في المنع من العدوّ أو السلطان نادر وعلى شرف الزوال فكان ملحقاً بالعدم والله تعالى أعلم انتهت. فقوله إذا لم يقدر على مجامعتها هو توفيق البدائع بين القولين. ووفق المقدسي في شرحه بوجه آخر أخذاً من قوله في الفتح والحبس بحق لا يعتبر في الفيء باللسان وبظلم يعتبر قوله: (وشمل كلامه ما إذا كان قادراً الخ) أي في أن فيئه الوطء وقوله «وما إذا كان عاجزاً وقته الخ» معطوف على قوله «ما إذا كان قادراً ففي الصورتين لا يكون فيئه باللسان. والحاصل أن شروط صحة الفيء باللسان ثلاثة : العجز عند الوطء، ودوامه من وقت الإيلاء إلى مضي المدة وبه صرح في الملتقى، وقيام النكاح وقت الفيء باللسان كما تقدم عن البدائع .
Intercourse; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. He restricted it by what he mentioned of the types of real incapacity as a precaution against legal incapacity, such as being in ihram at the time of ila' and between him and Hajj four months, so according to us his fay' is only by intercourse, because the one who causes [the incapacity] by his own choice through a prohibited means in what is binding upon him does not deserve leniency. He intended the fay' by speech to be considered as nullifying ila' regarding divorce, but regarding the remaining of the oath in terms of violation, no, so that if he had intercourse with her after fay' by speech within the period of ila', expiation becomes obligatory upon him due to the occurrence of violation. In al-Bada'i: Among the conditions for the validity of fay' by speech is the existence of the marriage bond at the time of fay' by speech, which is that at the time he makes fay' to her, his wife is not irrevocably separated from him, for if she is irrevocably separated and he makes fay' with his tongue, that is not fay' and ila' remains, because fay' by speech during the existence of marriage only removes ila' regarding the ruling of divorce by fulfilling her right with it, and she has no right when irrevocably separated, unlike fay' by intercourse, for it is valid after the establishment of irrevocable separation such that ila' does not remain but is nullified because he violated by intercourse so the oath is dissolved and nullified, and violation does not occur here so the oath is not dissolved and ila' is not removed. End. His statement: (And if he becomes able within the period, then his fay' is intercourse) because it is a substitute for it, so if he is able to do the original before achieving the purpose by the substitute, it becomes invalid, like the one performing tayammum if he sees water during his prayer. He restricted it to being within the period because if he becomes able after it, it does not become invalid. His statement includes the case where he was able at the time of ila' then became incapacitated, provided that time passes in which he could have intercourse with her after ila', and the case where he was incapacitated at that time then became able within the period. As for if he made a perpetual ila' while ill, then she becomes irrevocably separated after the period, then he recovers and marries her while ill, and he makes fay' with his tongue, it is not valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, and they corrected his statement; thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Jami' al-Kabir for al-Sadr: al-Jami' is an original and the tongue is its substitute. He made ila' in his illness and made fay' with his tongue; his ila' is nullified regarding divorce, but if he recovers before the end of the period, it becomes nullified because he is able to do the original, like the one performing tayammum. If he did not make fay' until she became irrevocably separated, then he recovered, then he fell ill and married her, then his fay' is by intercourse. From Abu Yusuf and Zufar because... al-Tahawi: that if he made ila' from his wife while she is imprisoned or he is imprisoned, or between him and his wife there is less than four months, except that the enemy or the sultan prevented him from that, then his fay' is only by action. And it is possible to reconcile the two statements regarding imprisonment by interpreting what the judge mentioned as the case where one of them can reach the other in prison, and the aspect of prevention by the enemy or sultan is rare and on the verge of disappearance, so it is treated as non-existent. And Allah knows best. Ended. So his statement: if he is unable to have intercourse with her is the reconciliation of al-Bada'i' between the two statements. And al-Maqdisi in his commentary reconciled in another way, taking from his statement in al-Fath: imprisonment by right is not considered for fay' by speech, but by oppression it is considered. His statement: (His statement includes the case where he was able, etc.) i.e., that his fay' is intercourse; and his statement 'and the case where he was incapacitated at the time, etc.' is conjoined with his statement 'the case where he was able'. So in both cases, his fay' is not by speech. The summary is that the conditions for the validity of fay' by speech are three: incapacity at the time of intercourse, its continuity from the time of ila' to the passage of the period, as stated in al-Multaqa, and the existence of marriage at the time of fay' by speech, as previously from al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the conditions under which al-fay' (return to marital relations) in ila' is valid by speech (الفيء باللسان) versus requiring actual intercourse. The text begins by distinguishing real incapacity (العجز الحقيقي) from legal incapacity (العجز الحكمي). For example, if a man is in ihram at the time of ila' and there are four months until Hajj, he cannot make fay' by speech; he must actually have intercourse because he caused his own incapacity through a prohibited act. The fay' by speech is effective only for the purpose of preventing divorce, not for expiating the oath. If he later has intercourse, he must pay kaffarah because he violated his oath. Quoting al-Bada'i', the text states that for fay' by speech to be valid, the marriage must still exist (the wife not being irrevocably divorced) at the time of the fay'. If the wife is irrevocably divorced, fay' by speech does not remove ila', whereas fay' by intercourse remains valid because it breaks the oath. The text then discusses the case where the husband becomes able to have intercourse during the ila' period: his fay' must then be by intercourse, using the analogy of tayammum — if water is found during prayer, tayammum is invalidated. Similarly, if he becomes able within the period, the substitute (speech) is invalid and he must perform the original (intercourse). The passage also covers various scenarios: perpetual ila' while ill, recovery, remarriage, and the views of Abu Yusuf and Zufar. It ends by summarizing the three conditions for valid fay' by speech: incapacity at the time of intercourse, continuous incapacity from ila' to the end of the period, and existence of marriage at the time of fay'.

Key Terms
الفيء (al-fay'): Return to marital relations with the wife after making a vow of abstinence (ila'), either by speech or by intercourse.
الإيلاء (al-ila'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife for four months or more.
العجز الحقيقي (al-'ajz al-haqiqi): Actual physical inability to have intercourse, as opposed to legal inability like being in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which of the following is NOT a condition for valid fay' by speech in ila' according to the passage?
A) The husband must be physically unable to have intercourse at the time of fay'.
B) The husband must have been continuously unable from the start of ila' to the end of the period.
C) The husband must have made ila' in a state of legal incapacity (ihram).
D) The marriage must still exist at the time of fay'.

Key Takeaway
Fay' by speech is only valid under the three conditions; otherwise actual intercourse is required.

Default Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on 'Anti Alayya Haram'
Ruling on the phrase 'you are haram to me' as ila' or zihar based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling for the phrase 'anti alayya haram', identify the condition that changes it to zihar, and explain the dispute between the two and Muhammad.

Core Idea
The phrase 'anti alayya haram' is ila' by default; only explicit intention for zihar makes it zihar.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a common but nuanced issue: when a husband says 'you are haram to me', what is the legal effect? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on intention, with a default classification that has practical consequences. We will parse the default rule, the exception for zihar, and the intra-school disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Ruling
What is the default legal classification of the phrase 'anti alayya haram' when the husband intends prohibition (tahrim) or no specific intention at all? The text states it is ila' (a vow of abstinence).
Ila' triggers a four-month waiting period and potential divorce if intercourse does not resume.
2
Recognize the Exception for Zihar
If the husband explicitly intends to declare zihar (comparing his wife to a forbidden relative), then the phrase becomes zihar according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
Zihar requires expiation (kaffarah) before intercourse can resume; it does not automatically divorce.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees with the two, arguing that zihar requires a specific resemblance to a mahram (e.g., 'you are like my mother'), which is absent in the mere word 'haram'.
The two respond that absolute prohibition (haram) can accommodate the qualified prohibition of zihar when intended.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: if it is zihar, the husband must perform expiation; if it remains ila' (as per Muhammad's view), the rules of ila' apply, which may lead to divorce after four months.
The preferred view in the school (mufta bihi) is that of the two, but later custom often treats such statements as divorce.
5
Note the Custom-Based Fatwa
The passage concludes that contemporary custom (urf) often leads to treating the phrase as a divorcing oath rather than ila' or zihar, so fatwa is given accordingly.
This reflects the principle that custom can change the interpretation of ambiguous statements.

Source Text

الوطء أنت علي حرام ايلاء إن نوى التحريم أو لم ينو شيئاً وظهار أن نواه وكذب أن حرام كالخلوة لكنه بتقصيره كمن أحرم بالحج ثم الى أو آلى وهو صحيح ثم بانت ثم مرض وتزوجها بخلاف إن تزوجتك فوالله لا أقربك . Intercourse you are haram to me is ila' if he intended prohibition or did not intend anything, and zihar if he intended it, and false that... haram like seclusion but he is negligent, like one who has entered ihram for Hajj then made ila', or made ila' while healthy then she became irrevocably divorced then he fell ill and married her, unlike 'if I marry you, by Allah I will not approach you'. He made ila' in his illness then repeated it after ten days and recovered in some of the period, so it is as before. End. His statement: ('You are haram to me' is ila' if he intended prohibition or did not intend anything) because the origin in making permissible things forbidden is only the oath according to us, as we will mention in the chapter on oaths, Allah willing. And there is no difference in all rulings between mentioning the word 'upon me' (علي) or not mentioning it. What he mentioned in Khizanat al-Akmal from al-Uyun that if he said 'you are haram' or 'ba'in' and did not say 'from me' (مني), it is invalid, is a mistake from him where he transmitted it from al-Uyun. In al-Uyun, that is mentioned from the wife's side: if he put the matter in her hand and she said to the husband 'you are haram to me' or 'you are from me ba'in' or 'haram' or 'I am haram to you' or 'ba'in', it occurs; and if she said 'you are ba'in' or 'haram' and did not say 'from me', it is invalid. And in some copies of al-Uyun: 'and if he said without the feminine taa', so the author of al-Akmal thought it was a new issue and thought that if that man said it to his wife, it is invalid. He said, may Allah be pleased with him: and with this the error of our shaykh Najm al-Din al-Bukhari increased, so he added the word 'to her' (لها) and said: if he said to her 'you are haram' or 'ba'in', it is invalid. And the issue with the feminine taa' is mentioned in al-Waqi'at al-Kubra, both ordered and unordered, in the issues of al-Uyun, so his error and the other's is known by this; thus in al-Qunyah. He restricted it to the husband because if the wife says to her husband 'I am haram to you' or 'I have made you haram', it becomes an oath, so that if he has intercourse with her willingly or unwillingly, he violates, unlike if he swore not to enter this house and then was forced into it unwillingly, he does not violate, meaning he was carried in; and if he was forced to enter and entered unwillingly, he violates; thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. And 'I have made you haram to me' or if he did not say 'upon me' (علي), or 'you are forbidden to me' (محرمة) or 'haram to me' or did not say 'upon me', or 'I am haram to you' or 'forbidden' or 'I have made myself haram to you' is like 'you are haram to me' as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And his statement 'you are to me like a donkey or pig or anything that is haram in essence' is like his statement 'you are haram to me' as in al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: ('and zihar if he intended it') i.e., zihar, and this is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad said: it is not zihar because of the lack of resemblance to a forbidden woman, which is the pillar in it. And for the two: he declared absolute prohibition and in zihar there is a type of prohibition, and the absolute may carry the qualified; thus in al-Hidayah following al-Quduri and Shams al-A'imma. And the disagreement is not mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah; so al-Hakim al-Shahid did not mention it in his Mukhtasar, nor al-Tahawi. His statement: ('and there is consideration, etc.') it is not hidden that divorce is an oath, and therefore they said: it is disliked to swear by divorce, so the oath is more general than its cause being kaffarah or divorce. And that which is acted upon and given fatwa is a specific type of this oath, which is its application to divorce. Also, its being an oath is an original custom, and its being divorce is a later custom, and there is no doubt that the speech of every contractor, swearer, and the like is interpreted according to his custom, as mentioned in al-Ashbah. And since there is a custom, its literal meaning is not intended; so intending a lie is contrary to the apparent, so it is not believed judicially. So the correct view is to interpret it according to custom, but since the custom
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal effect of a husband's statement 'أنت علي حرام' (you are haram to me). The text explains that if the husband intended prohibition (تحريم) or no specific intention, the default ruling is that it constitutes ila' (a vow of abstinence), binding him to the rules of ila'. However, if he explicitly intended zihar (a specific form of divorce-like prohibition), then it is zihar. This is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Imam Muhammad, however, disagrees and says it cannot be zihar because zihar requires a specific comparison to a forbidden relative (like a mother), and the phrase lacks that resemblance. The two argue that the phrase implies absolute prohibition, and zihar includes a type of prohibition, so the general may include the specific. The text then notes that this disagreement is not in Zahir al-Riwayah, meaning it is not from the foundational books of the school. The passage also discusses related variations of wording, such as adding 'علي' or not, and analogies like comparing to a donkey or pig. It clarifies that this ruling is specific to the husband; if the wife says it, it becomes an oath. The text ends with a discussion on custom and divorce, noting that the prevalent custom (urf) nowadays is for such statements to be treated as divorce rather than a general oath, so fatwa is given accordingly.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-ila'): A vow of abstinence from intercourse, which the phrase 'anti alayya haram' constitutes by default.
الظهار (al-zihar): A form of divorce by comparing the wife to a mahram relative, requiring expiation.
التحريم (al-tahrim): Intention to make intercourse forbidden, which triggers ila' in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), what is the ruling if a husband says 'anti alayya haram' and intends zihar?
A) It is ila'.
B) It is zihar.
C) It is a binding divorce.
D) It is an ordinary oath requiring expiation.

Key Takeaway
The default classification of 'anti alayya haram' is ila'; only explicit intention for zihar (according to the two) changes it to zihar, and later custom may treat it as divorce.

Talaq — Divorce

Custom as Proof: Effect of 'Anti 'Alayya Haram' Without Intention
Discusses the fatwa that if 'haram' is commonly used for divorce, it falls qada'an without intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why custom (urf) causes a divorce to occur qada'an when a husband says 'anti 'alayya haram' without specific intention, and distinguish the judicial ruling from the religious reality (diyana).

Core Idea
Custom can make an oath of 'haram' function as a divorce in court, even without the husband's intention.

In everyday life, people may use the phrase 'you are haram to me' as a way to divorce their wives. But what if the husband does not actually intend divorce? The Hanafi school, guided by custom, rules that the divorce occurs in the eyes of the court (qada'an), even though between him and Allah (diyanatan) it may not count. This lesson unpacks the fatwa that relies on custom to effect a ba'in divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling and Its Basis
The passage states that if a husband says 'anti 'alayya haram' and custom uses this for divorce, divorce falls judicially (qada'an) even without intention. Identify the role of custom as the proof (dalil).
The fatwa is based on urf, not explicit Quran or Sunnah.
2
Understand the Qada'/Diyana Distinction
The author qualifies the ruling with 'qada'an' to emphasize that diyanatan (religious reality) requires intention. Note the difference: court ruling vs. inner state.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyse the Role of Custom as Evidence
Custom is a zanni (speculative) source of law. The Hanafi school gives it weight when it is widespread and consistent. Explain how custom overrides the original meaning of the phrase as an oath.
The Phrase is originally an oath (yamin), but custom reinterprets it as talaq.
4
Review the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Multiple Wives
The passage notes disagreement: al-Nasafi (no kaffarah), Shaykh al-Islam al-Uzjandi (one wife), and al-Fatawa (each wife). The author prefers the fatwa view as more closely aligned with the encompassing phrase.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate and leads to different practical outcomes.
5
Apply to Similar Phrases
The ruling extends to phrases like 'anti ma'i fi al-haram' and 'halal al-muslimin 'alayya haram'. Apply the same logic: custom determines the effect.
If the husband has no wife, the oath triggers kaffarah if broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وفي الفتاوى إذا قال لامرأته أنت علي حرام والحرام عنده طلاق ولكن لم ينو طلاقاً وقع الطلاق) قضاء لما ظهر من العرف في حتى لو قال لامرأته إن تزوجتك فحلال الله علي حرام فتزوجها تطلق ولهذا لا يحلف به إلا الرجال قيدنا بالقضاء لأنه لا يقع الطلاق ديانة بلا نية. وذكر الإمام ظهير الدين: لا نقول لا تشترط النية لكن يجعل ناوياً عرفاً. فإن قلت: إذا وقع الطلاق بلا نية ينبغي أن يكون كالصريح فيكون الواقع رجعياً قلت : المتعارف به إيقاع البائن ؛ كذا في البزازية. فلو قال المصنف ويقع البائن لكان أولى . وقوله أنت معي في الحرام بمنزلة قوله أنت علي حرام وكذا قوله حلال المسلمين علي حرام. وفي المواضع التي يقع الطلاق بلفظ الحرام إن لم تكن له امرأة إن حنث لزمته الكفارة. والنسفي على أنه لا تلزمه وإن كان له أكثر من زوجة واحدة قال في الفتاوى : يقع على كل تطليقة واحدة بخلاف الصريح فإنه لا يقع إلا واحدة فيما إذا قال امرأته طالق وله أكثر من واحدة. وأجاب شيخ الإسلام الاوز جندي أنه لا يقع إلا على واحدة وإليه البيان وهو الأشبه؛ كذا في البزازية والخلاصة والذخيرة وفي فتح القدير وعندي أن الأشبه ما في الفتاوى لأن قوله حلال الله أو حلال المسلمين يعم كل زوجة فإذا كان فيه عرف في الطلاق
His statement: (And in the Fatawa: if he said to his wife 'anti 'alayya haram' and 'haram' in his view is divorce, but he did not intend divorce, divorce occurs) judicially, due to what has appeared of custom, so that even if he said to his wife 'if I marry you, then what Allah has made halal is haram for me' and then marries her, she is divorced. For this reason, only men swear by it. We qualified it with 'judicially' because divorce does not occur diyanatan without intention. Imam Zahir al-Din mentioned: We do not say intention is not a condition, but he is considered as intending customarily. If you say: if divorce occurs without intention, it should be like explicit (sarih) and thus the occurring would be raj'i. I say: what is customary is to effect ba'in; as in al-Bazzaziyya. And his statement: 'you are with me in haram' is like his statement 'anti 'alayya haram'; similarly his statement 'the halal of Muslims is haram on me'. And in situations where divorce occurs by the word 'haram', if he has no wife, if he breaks the oath, he incurs kaffarah. And al-Nasafi holds that it is not incumbent, and if he has more than one wife, it is said in al-Fatawa: it occurs as one talaq for each, unlike the explicit (sarih) which only occurs as one when he says 'his wife is divorced' and he has more than one wife. Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa discussion regarding the effect of custom (urf) on the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram'. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn's mention of a fatwa. The fatwa states that if the husband says 'anti 'alayya haram' and in his mind 'haram' means divorce, but he does not specifically intend divorce, divorce still occurs in court (qada'an) because the common custom among people is to use this phrase for divorce. The qualification 'qada'an' is important: it means that the judge rules that the divorce has occurred based on apparent custom, but between him and Allah (diyanatan) it does not count unless he intended it. This is similar to the case where a man says 'if I marry you, what Allah has made halal is haram on me' and then marries, she is divorced. Imam Zahir al-Din explains that it is not that intention is not required, but that the husband is considered to have intended it customarily. There is a question: if divorce occurs without intention, why is it not like explicit (sarih) and thus raj'i? The answer: the custom is such that people intend ba'in (irrevocable) divorce when using this phrase. The passage then extends the ruling to similar phrases like 'anti ma'i fi al-haram' (you are with me in the forbidden) and 'halal al-muslimin 'alayya haram' (the lawful of Muslims is forbidden to me). If a man has no wife and uses such an oath, breaking it requires kaffarah. If he has more than one wife, the fatwa says it falls as one talaq for each wife, unlike explicit divorce which applies only to one. However, Shaykh al-Islam al-Uzjandi holds that it applies only to one wife, but the author finds the fatwa view more similar to the evidence because the phrase encompasses all wives, and custom supports it.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyānatan): Between the husband and Allah; the internal, spiritual reality of the act, as opposed to the external judicial ruling (qada'an).
قضاء (qaḍā'an): Judicially; the ruling given by a judge based on apparent evidence, which may differ from the inner reality.
رجعي (raj'ī): A revocable divorce during the iddah period; the husband may take back his wife without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'anti 'alayya haram' without intending divorce, but the common custom is to use this phrase for divorce, what is the ruling judicially (qada'an)?
A) No divorce occurs because intention is required.
B) A ba'in divorce occurs.
C) A raj'i divorce occurs.
D) The husband must give a kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Custom can cause a phrase to function as a divorce qada'an even without intention, resulting in a ba'in divorce, but the religious reality (diyana) depends on actual intention.

The Legal Effect of 'Anti 'Alayya Haram' in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary expanding the ruling on a kinaya becoming sarih by custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram' effects a ba'in divorce judicially without intention, and distinguish the rulings in qada' and diyanah.

Core Idea
Custom transforms an ambiguous phrase into an explicit divorce, but only externally: diyanah still requires intention.

In Hanafi fiqh, the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram' has evolved from a kinaya (indirect expression) to what custom treats as an explicit divorce pronouncement. This lesson unpacks the legal mechanism: how custom (urf) elevates the ruling, why divorce occurs without articulated intention in court (qada'), yet religiously (diyanah) intention remains necessary. We will also examine why the divorce is considered ba'in (irrevocable) rather than raj'i, a point of reflection among later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its legal status in early Hanafi doctrine
Start by recognizing that 'anti 'alayya haram' ('you are forbidden to me') was originally a kinaya for divorce, requiring intention (niyyah) to effect a divorce. The base matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) treats it as such.
Kinaya rulings: the pronouncement is not explicit in meaning; its effect depends on intention.
2
Understand the role of custom (urf) in changing the ruling
Note that later Hanafi fatwa (al-Fatawa) declared that due to widespread custom, this phrase is now treated like a sarih (explicit) pronouncement. The proof is urf, not a direct text. The commentary states: 'qada'an lima zahara min al-urf' – judicially, because of what appeared from custom.
Custom is a recognized source of law (dalil) in Hanafi usul when it is prevailing and not contradicting a definitive text.
3
Distinguish between judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) rulings
In qada' (court ruling), divorce occurs even if the husband did not intend divorce, because custom imputes intention to him. In diyanah (before Allah), intention is still required: if he truly did not intend divorce, the divorce is not valid religiously. This is a key distinction in Hanafi law for ambiguous pronouncements.
The text says: 'qayyadna bi al-qada'' (we restricted it to the judicial) because it does not occur diyanatan without niyyah.
4
Determine the type of divorce: ba'in vs. raj'i
The custom, according to al-Bazaziyya, is to effect a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce, not raj'i. The commentator notes that if it were truly sarih, it should be raj'i by default, but the custom dictates ba'in. He also reflects that in his own time commoners use the phrase without distinguishing, so perhaps it should be raj'i. The fatwa, however, remains ba'in.
Ba'in: the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage. Raj'i: revocable during the waiting period.
5
Recognize the authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin
The ruling that 'anti 'alayya haram' effects ba'in divorce without intention in qada' is the mufta bihi (operative position) among later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin). The source cites al-Bazaziyya, Fath al-Qadir, and others.
Authority_weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin, meaning the position is based on later fatwa, not necessarily on the earliest texts (zahir al-riwayah).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
