الطلاق بلفظ الحرام إن لم تكن له امرأة إن حنث لزمته الكفارة. والنسفي على أنه لا تلزمه وإن كان له أكثر من زوجة واحدة قال في الفتاوى : يقع على كل تطليقة واحدة بخلاف الصريح فإنه لا يقع إلا واحدة فيما إذا قال امرأته طالق وله أكثر من واحدة. وأجاب شيخ الإسلام الاوز جندي أنه لا يقع إلا على واحدة وإليه البيان وهو الأشبه ؛ كذا في البزازية والخلاصة والذخيرة وفي فتح القدير وعندي أن الأشبه ما في الفتاوى لأن قوله حلال الله أو حلال المسلمين يعم كل زوجة فإذا كان فيه عرف في الطلاق الاستعمال (قيدنا بالقضاء الخ) أقول : حيث التحق في العرف بالصريح لم يحتج إلى نية بل يحتاج إلى عدم نية الطلاق مما يحتمله لفظه كما لو نوى بأنت طالق عن وثاق كما تقدم بيانه أول الطلاق قوله : (قلت المتعارف به إيقاع البائن أقول : كان هذا متعارف زمانهم أما في زماننا فعامة من يحلف به العوام وهم لا يميزون بين البائن والرجعي فضلاً أن عن يقصدوا به البائن فحيث كان بمنزلة الصريح بسبب غلبة في الطلاق وقلنا بوقوعه بلا نية للعرف ينبغي وقوع الرجعي به فليتأمل. وقد يقال : إنه وإن صار في العرف صريحاً لكن لفظه لا يحتمل وقوع الرجعي لأن كونها حراماً عليه يقتضي عدم حل قربانها والرجعي لا يحرم الوطء كما مر ولا يجعل إيلاء لأنه تحريم مع قيام العقد والعرف إرادة الحرمة بالطلاق، ولا ينافي وقوع البائن به مع كونه صريحاً لأن الصريح قد يقع به البائن كتطليقة شديدة كما أن بعض الكنايات يقع بها الرجعي كاعتدي واستبرئي رحمك وأنت واحدة فليتأمل قوله : (وفي فتح القدير وعندي أن الأشبه الخ قال في النهر وأقول : هذا لا يتم في قوله أنت علي حرام مخاطباً لواحدة كما قال المصنف وقول الشارح ولو كان له أربع نسوة والمسألة بحالها يقع على كل واحدة طلقة بائنة وقيل تطلق واحدة منهن وإليه البيان وهو الأظهر والأشبه» يجب أن يكون معناه والمسألة بحالها يعني في التحريم لا بقيد أنت كما لا يخفى بل في هذا يحب أن لا يقع إلا على المخاطبة اهـ. ومثله في منح الغفار من بحث الصريح والشرنبلالية وفي العزمية على الدرر والغرر . كما يكون بمنزلة قوله هن طوالق لأن حلال الله يشملهن على سبيل الاستغراق لا على سبيل البدل في قوله إحداكن طالق، وحيث وقع الطلاق بهذا اللفظ وقع بائناً اهـ. ويوجد في بعض النسخ وفي الفتاوى وفي بعضها وفي الفتوى والأولى لا يدل على أنه أن هذا هو المفتى به مع القول هو المفتى به عند المتأخرين ولذا قال في البزازية ومشايخنا أفتوا في أنه لو قال أنت علي حرام والحلال عليه حرام أو حلال الله عليه حرام أو حلال المسلمين عليه حرام أن الكل بائن بلا نية وإذا حلف بهذه الألفاط على فعل في المستقبل ففعل وليست له امرأة عليه الكفارة، وإذا كان له امرأة وقت الحلف وماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط الصحيح أنه لا تطلق امرأته المتزوجة وعليه الفتوى لأن حلفه صار حلفاً بالله تعالى وقت الوجود فلا ينقلب طلاقاً خالعها ثم قال حلال الله علي حرام إن شرب إلى سنة وشرب لا يقع لعدم الملك والإضافة إليه، ولو قال لها إن تزوجتك فحلال الله علي حرام فتزوجها تطلق . قال بعضهم : والصحيح خلافه لوقوعه على القائمة لا على المتزوجة فلو لم تكن في نكاحه وقت وجود الشرط امرأة لا يقع على فلانة أيضاً وتمامه في البزازية. وفي قوله حلال الله عليه حرام وله امرأتان ولم تكن له نية طلقتا وإن نوى إحداهما دين لا في القضاء، وفتوى الإمام الاوزجندي على أنه يقع على واحدة وعليه البيان وقد ذكرناه. وفي الظهيرية : حلف بهذه الألفاظ أنه لم يفعل كذا وكان فعله وله امرأتان وأكثر بن، وإن ليست له امرأة فلا لم : ولعل مراد الزيلعي بكون المسألة بحالها هو أن يكون الحرام عنده طلاقاً، وأما كون المسألة في تلك الصورة على أن يقال أنت علي حرام فليس بداخل في ذلك فإن ما يقتضيه صحة المساق هو أن تكون العبارة ها هنا امرأتي علي حرام إذ لا مساغ لأن يقال لأربع نسوة أنت علي حرام ولا تتأتى صحة القولين المذكورين إلا على ما قررنا .اهـ.. لكن في قوله أن تكون العبارة ها هنا امرأتي علي حرام» نظر والظاهر إبداله بحلال الله أو حلال المسلمين لما ذكره المؤلف هنا عن الفتاوى من أن قوله امرأتي طالق وله أكثر من واحدة لا يقع إلا على واحدة، ولم يحكوا في هذا خلافاً بل ظاهر قوله بخلاف الصريح أنها اتفاقية كما ذكره في منح الغفار راداً على الدرر في ذكره التصحيح في الصريح أيضاً. وحينئذ فلا فرق فيما يظهر بين امرأتي طالق وبين امرأتي علي حرام في كونه لا يشمل غير واحدة فيما لو كان له أكثر إلا أن يوجد نقل بخلافه فيتبع فجعل العزمية محل الخلاف امرأتي حرام في كونه يقع على الكل أو على واحدة غير ظاهر. فالحاصل أن المخرج من محل النزاع أنت علي حرام كما مر وامرأتي علي حرام فتأمل وراجع وانظر في تعليل الفتح يتقوى عندك ما قلنا قوله: (ويوجد في بعض النسخ) أقول : يؤيد النسخة الثانية ما سيذكره المصنف. متناً في الأيمان كل حل عليه حرام فهو على الطعام والشراب، والفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته من غير نية. قال المؤلف هناك في شرحه لغلبة الاستعمال؛ كذا في الهداية . وإن لم تكن له امرأة ذكر في النهاية معزياً إلى النوازل أنه تجب عليه الكفارة اهـ. يعني
And upon them both [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], and according to them as he intended. He said to three [wives]: 'You are forbidden to me,' and he intended three for the first, and the oath for the second, and lying for the third, then she is divorced three times. And it is said that this is according to the second [Imam], and according to them it should be as he intended. That is it. His statement: (And in al-Fatawa: if he said to his wife 'You are forbidden to me' and al-haram according to him is divorce but he did not intend divorce, the divorce occurs) judicially because of what appeared from custom, such that even if he said to his wife 'If I marry you, then what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me' and he marries her, she is divorced. And for this reason none swear by it except men. We restricted it to the judicial because divorce does not occur religiously without intention. Imam Zahir al-Din mentioned: We do not say that intention is not required, but he is considered as intending customarily. If you say: When divorce occurs without intention, it should be like explicit [sarih] and so the occurring [divorce] should be raj'i, I say: The customary practice is to effect a ba'in divorce; thus in al-Bazaziyya. So if the author had said 'and it occurs as ba'in' it would have been better. And his statement 'You are with me in prohibition' is like his statement 'You are forbidden to me', and likewise his statement 'What the Muslims have made lawful is forbidden to me'. And in the places where divorce occurs with the word 'haram', if he has no woman [wife] then if he breaks [his oath], expiation is obligatory upon him. Al-Nasafi holds that it is not obligatory. And if he has more than one wife, he said in al-Fatawa: it falls on each one as a single divorce, unlike the explicit [phrase] which does not fall except as one when he says 'my wife is divorced' and he has more than one. And Shaykh al-Islam al-Auzjandi answered that it does not fall except on one, and to him is the clarification and it is the most similar; thus in al-Bazaziyya, al-Khulasa, and al-Dhakhira, and in Fath al-Qadir: and according to me, the most similar is what is in al-Fatawa because his statement 'What Allah has made lawful' or 'What the Muslims have made lawful' encompasses every wife. So if there is a custom in [such] use regarding divorce... (We restricted to the judicial etc.) I say: When it is joined to the explicit by custom, it does not need intention, but rather it needs the absence of intention of divorce that its wording can bear, as if he intended by 'you are divorced' [meaning] from bondage, as was explained at the beginning of divorce. His statement (I said: The customary practice is to effect a ba'in divorce) I say: This was the customary practice of their time, but in our time, most commoners who swear by it do not distinguish between ba'in and raj'i, let alone intending ba'in. So when it is like the explicit due to predominance in divorce, and we say it occurs without intention due to custom, it should occur as raj'i, so let it be considered. And it may be said: Even if it has become explicit by custom, its wording does not bear the occurrence of raj'i because that she is forbidden to him requires the illegality of approaching her, and the raj'i does not prohibit intercourse, as has passed, and it is not made ila' because it is prohibition while the marriage contract is in effect, and custom intends prohibition by divorce. And it does not contradict the occurrence of ba'in by it while it is explicit, because the explicit may sometimes yield ba'in, like a severe divorce, just as some kinayat yield raj'i, like 'consider yourself divorced' and 'seek to know your womb' and 'you are one'. So let it be considered. His statement: (And in Fath al-Qadir: and according to me, the most similar etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And I say: This is not complete in the statement 'You are forbidden to me' when addressing one, as the author said, and the commentator's statement 'and if he had four wives and the case as it is, it falls on each one as a ba'in divorce, and it is said that only one of them is divorced, and to him is the clarification and it is the most apparent and most similar' must mean that the case as it is means in prohibition, not with the restriction 'you', as is not hidden; rather in this it must not occur except on the addressed. End. And similar is in Manh al-Ghaffar from the discussion of sarih and al-Sharnablaliyya and in al-Azmiyya on al-Durar wa al-Ghurar. As it is like his statement 'They are all divorced' because 'what Allah has made lawful' encompasses them on the basis of inclusiveness, not on the basis of substitution like his statement 'One of you is divorced', and when divorce occurs by this wording, it occurs as ba'in. End. And it is found in some copies 'and in al-Fatawa' and in some copies 'and in al-Fatwa' and the first does not indicate that this is the mufta bihi, although the latter is the mufta bihi according to the later scholars. Therefore it is said in al-Bazaziyya: Our shaykhs issued fatwa that if he said 'You are forbidden to me' and 'what is lawful is forbidden to him' or 'what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to him' or 'what the Muslims have made lawful is forbidden to him', all are ba'in without intention. And if he swears by these phrases on a future action, then he does it, and he has no wife, then expiation is due upon him. And if he had a wife at the time of the oath, and she died before the condition or separated without a waiting period, then he fulfills the condition, the correct view is that his married wife is not divorced, and upon it is the fatwa, because his oath became an oath by Allah Almighty at the time of existence, so it does not turn into divorce. If he divorced her [khala'aha], then said 'What Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me if I drink for a year', and he drinks, it does not occur [as divorce] due to lack of ownership and attribution to her. And if he said to her 'If I marry you, then what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me', and he marries her, she is divorced. Some said: The correct is the opposite, because it occurs on the existing one, not on the one married. So if there was no woman in his marriage at the time of the condition's existence, it does not occur on such-and-such either. And its completion is in al-Bazaziyya. And in the statement 'What Allah has made lawful is forbidden to him' and he has two wives and he had no intention, both are divorced; and if he intended one of them, it is religiously [not judicially] [firm]. And the fatwa of Imam al-Auzjandi is that it falls on one, and the clarification has been mentioned. And in al-Zahiriyya: He swore by these words that he did not do such-and-such, and he did it, and he has two wives or more... and if he has no wife, then not... Perhaps al-Zayla'i's intent with 'the case as it is' is that haram according to him is divorce, and as for the case in that form that he says 'You are forbidden to me', that is not included in that, because what the correctness of the context requires is that the expression here should be 'My wife is forbidden to me', since it is not permissible to say to four women 'You are forbidden to me', and the correctness of the two mentioned statements is not possible except on what we have established. End. But in his statement 'the expression here should be 'My wife is forbidden to me'' there is consideration, and the apparent is to replace it with 'What Allah has made lawful' or 'What the Muslims have made lawful', because what the author mentioned here from al-Fatawa that his statement 'My wife is divorced' and he has more than one does not fall except on one, and they have not reported a difference in this, rather the apparent from his statement 'unlike the explicit' is that it is agreed upon, as mentioned in Manh al-Ghaffar in refutation of al-Durar in its mentioning the correction regarding the explicit as well. Therefore, there is no difference in what appears between 'My wife is divorced' and 'My wife is forbidden to me' in that it does not include more than one when he has more, unless a transmission to the contrary is found, then it is to be followed. So making al-Azmiyya the place of dispute about 'My wife is forbidden to me' concerning whether it falls on all or one is not apparent. So the summary is that the exit from the place of contention is 'You are forbidden to me' as has passed, and 'My wife is forbidden to me'. So reflect, refer, and look at the explanation of Fath al-Qadir, and what we said will become strong for you. His statement: (And it is found in some copies) I say: The second copy is supported by what the author will mention as matn in the chapter on oaths: 'Everything that is lawful is forbidden to him' it is regarding food and drink, and the fatwa is that his wife becomes separated from him without intention. The author said there in his commentary due to predominance of usage; similarly in al-Hidayah. And if he has no wife, it is mentioned in al-Nihaya citing al-Nawazil that expiation becomes obligatory upon him. End. Meaning
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling of saying 'anti 'alayya haram' (you are forbidden to me) in the context of divorce. The passage addresses multiple dimensions: the legal nature of the phrase, the requirement of intention, the difference between judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) rulings, the type of divorce (ba'in vs. raj'i), and the effect when multiple wives are involved.

The text begins by continuing a discussion about a man saying 'antunna 'alayya haram' to three wives with specific intentions. It then moves to the main point: according to the fatwa, if a man says to his wife 'anti 'alayya haram' and in his understanding 'haram' means divorce, but he did not intend divorce, the divorce occurs judicially due to the prevalent custom. This is because the phrase has become so commonly used for divorce that it is treated like an explicit pronouncement (sarih). However, this is only in the judicial sphere (qada'); religiously (diyanah), divorce does not occur without intention. Imam Zahir al-Din explains that we do not say intention is unnecessary, but rather the man is deemed to have intended divorce by custom. When it becomes like sarih, intention is not needed, but one must ensure he did not intend something other than divorce that the wording could bear.

The commentary then discusses the type of divorce: the custom was to effect a ba'in divorce, so the ruling is that it falls as ba'in. However, the commentator notes that in his own time, commoners who use this phrase do not distinguish between ba'in and raj'i, and argues that since it has become like sarih due to custom, it should be raj'i. He reflects that the wording 'haram' implies complete prohibition, which seems to fit ba'in, yet some kinaya (indirect) phrases yield raj'i. He leaves this for consideration.

The passage then addresses the issue of multiple wives: if a man says 'halal Allah 'alayya haram' and has more than one wife, the fatwa in al-Fatawa states that it falls on each wife as one divorce, unlike the explicit 'my wife is divorced' which falls only on one. However, Shaykh al-Islam al-Auzjandi held that it falls only on one. The commentator in Fath al-Qadir prefers the fatwa because phrases like 'halal Allah' or 'halal al-muslimin' are general and cover all wives. But there is also a view that when addressing a specific wife, it only applies to her.

The text further discusses cases where the husband has no wife at the time of the oath: if he breaks the oath, expiation (kaffarah) is due. It also clarifies that if he had a wife at the time of swearing but she dies or separates before the condition, the ruling is that the new wife he marries later is not divorced; the oath is considered an oath by Allah and does not turn into a divorce.

Finally, the commentary mentions a difference in manuscripts regarding the phrase 'in al-Fatawa' vs 'in al-Fatwa', and notes that the relied-upon position among later scholars is that all such phrases (anti 'alayya haram, halal Allah, halal al-muslimin) effect a ba'in divorce without intention. The passage concludes by relating this to the chapter on oaths, where 'halal 'alayya haram' is interpreted as referring to food and drink unless the context indicates divorce.

Throughout, the commentator engages with earlier authorities like al-Bazaziyya, Fath al-Qadir, al-Nahr, and others, showing the evolution of the fatwa within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
حرام (haram): In this context, the word 'haram' (forbidden) is used as a pronouncement of divorce, either as a kinaya (indirect) or, due to custom, as a sarih (explicit) declaration.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without the possibility of revocation (ruju'). The customary divorce by 'haram' is ruled ba'in in the fatwa.
قضاء (qada'): The judicial ruling or court decision. The phrase effects divorce in qada' due to custom even without intention.
ديانة (diyanah): Religiously, before Allah; the ruling based on the actual inner state. In diyanah, intention is required for divorce by 'haram'.
نية (niyyah): Intention. In divorce by kinaya, intention is normally required. However, due to custom, the phrase 'haram' has become so linked to divorce that intention is deemed present by custom in qada'.
عرف (urf): Custom or prevailing practice. Urf is the basis for treating the kinaya as sarih in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for treating 'anti 'alayya haram' as a sarih (explicit) divorce pronouncement in the judicial sphere?
A) A direct Quranic verse
B) A hadith of the Prophet (saw)
C) The prevailing custom (urf) of using the phrase for divorce
D) The consensus of all four madhhabs

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'anti 'alayya haram' effects a ba'in divorce judicially without intention due to custom, but religiously intention remains required.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
9 min
Applying 'Haram' Divorce to Multiple Wives: Internal Hanafi Dispute
Internal Hanafi dispute on applying general divorce phrases to multiple wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two views on whether phrases like 'halal Allah 'alayya haram' divorce all wives or only one, and identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to later scholars.

Core Idea
Generality vs. specificity: when a husband uses a general phrase like 'halal Allah ', does divorce strike all wives or just the addressed one? Later Hanafi fatwa leans toward the addressed wife.

When a husband utters a divorce phrase like 'halal Allah 'alayya haram' and has multiple wives, a dispute arises: does the divorce apply to all wives or only one? The passage presents two views: the earlier fatwa in al-Fatawa holds it applies to all (each gets one divorce), while Shaykh al-Islam al-Auzjandi holds it applies only to one. This lesson explores the reasoning behind each view and how later scholars attempted to reconcile them, focusing on the distinction between general phrases and specific addresses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario and the two rulings
The scenario: a man with multiple wives says 'halal Allah 'alayya haram' or a similar phrase like 'halal al-muslimin 'alayya haram'. Does it divorce all wives or only one? According to al-Fatawa, it falls on each wife as one divorce. According to al-Auzjandi, it falls only on one. Note that this is an internal Hanafi dispute.
The phrase 'halal Allah' is general, unlike 'anti 'alayya haram' which is specific to the addressed wife.
2
Analyze the proof for the view that it applies to all wives
The proof: 'halal Allah' or 'halal al-muslimin' is a general phrase that encompasses all wives, like saying 'they are all divorced'. The commentary in Fath al-Qadir prefers this because the phrase is inclusive (istighraq), not a substitution (badal). The generality of the wording triggers divorce for every wife.
The Hanafi principle: a general statement ('amm) is binding on all its instances unless specified otherwise.
3
Analyze the proof for the view that it applies to only one wife
Al-Auzjandi's reasoning: the phrase is essentially an oath by Allah, and when the condition is fulfilled, it does not turn into divorce for all. Alternatively, if the husband addressed a particular wife (even implicitly), the divorce is limited to her. The text also notes that if he says 'my wife is divorced' (sarih), it applies only to one, so by analogy 'my wife is forbidden' should also be one.
The analogy to sarih (explicit) divorce: when a man says 'my wife is divorced' with multiple wives, only one is divorced. Some argue the same should apply to 'haram'.
4
Determine the tarjih (preferment) and the fatwa today
The source mentions that the later scholars (mutakhkhirun) adopted the view of al-Auzjandi that it falls on one wife, not all. This is the 'clarification' (al-bayan) and 'the most similar' (al-ashbah). However, Fath al-Qadir and al-Bazaziyya support the general view. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq leaves it open, but notes the fatwa of al-Auzjandi is relied upon.
The mufta bihi is that when addressing a specific wife ('anti' phrase), it applies only to her. For general phrases, there is ikhtilaf; the later fatwa favors one.

Source Text

عليهما، وعندهما كما نوى. قال لثلاث أنتن علي حرام ونوى الثلاث في الواحدة واليمين في الثانية والكذب في الثالثة طلقت ثلاثاً، وقيل هذا على قول الثاني، وعلى قولهما ينبغي أن يكون على ما نوى اهـ . يعني ذلك قوله: (وفي الفتاوى إذا قال لامرأته أنت علي حرام والحرام عنده طلاق ولكن لم ينو طلاقاً وقع الطلاق) قضاء لما ظهر من العرف في حتى لو قال لامرأته إن تزوجتك فحلال الله علي حرام فتزوجها تطلق ولهذا لا يحلف به إلا الرجال قيدنا بالقضاء لأنه لا يقع الطلاق ديانة بلا نية. وذكر الإمام ظهير الدين: لا نقول لا تشترط النية لكن يجعل ناوياً عرفاً. فإن قلت: إذا وقع الطلاق بلا نية ينبغي أن يكون كالصريح فيكون الواقع رجعياً قلت : المتعارف به إيقاع البائن ؛ كذا في البزازية. فلو قال المصنف ويقع البائن لكان أولى . وقوله أنت معي في الحرام بمنزلة قوله أنت علي حرام وكذا قوله حلال المسلمين علي حرام. وفي المواضع التي يقع الطلاق بلفظ الحرام إن لم تكن له امرأة إن حنث لزمته الكفارة. والنسفي على أنه لا تلزمه وإن كان له أكثر من زوجة واحدة قال في الفتاوى : يقع على كل تطليقة واحدة بخلاف الصريح فإنه لا يقع إلا واحدة فيما إذا قال امرأته طالق وله أكثر من واحدة. وأجاب شيخ الإسلام الاوز جندي أنه لا يقع إلا على واحدة وإليه البيان وهو الأشبه ؛ كذا في البزازية والخلاصة والذخيرة وفي فتح القدير وعندي أن الأشبه ما في الفتاوى لأن قوله حلال الله أو حلال المسلمين يعم كل زوجة فإذا كان فيه عرف في الطلاق الاستعمال (قيدنا بالقضاء الخ) أقول : حيث التحق في العرف بالصريح لم يحتج إلى نية بل يحتاج إلى عدم نية الطلاق مما يحتمله لفظه كما لو نوى بأنت طالق عن وثاق كما تقدم بيانه أول الطلاق قوله : (قلت المتعارف به إيقاع البائن أقول : كان هذا متعارف زمانهم أما في زماننا فعامة من يحلف به العوام وهم لا يميزون بين البائن والرجعي فضلاً أن عن يقصدوا به البائن فحيث كان بمنزلة الصريح بسبب غلبة في الطلاق وقلنا بوقوعه بلا نية للعرف ينبغي وقوع الرجعي به فليتأمل. وقد يقال : إنه وإن صار في العرف صريحاً لكن لفظه لا يحتمل وقوع الرجعي لأن كونها حراماً عليه يقتضي عدم حل قربانها والرجعي لا يحرم الوطء كما مر ولا يجعل إيلاء لأنه تحريم مع قيام العقد والعرف إرادة الحرمة بالطلاق، ولا ينافي وقوع البائن به مع كونه صريحاً لأن الصريح قد يقع به البائن كتطليقة شديدة كما أن بعض الكنايات يقع بها الرجعي كاعتدي واستبرئي رحمك وأنت واحدة فليتأمل قوله : (وفي فتح القدير وعندي أن الأشبه الخ قال في النهر وأقول : هذا لا يتم في قوله أنت علي حرام مخاطباً لواحدة كما قال المصنف وقول الشارح ولو كان له أربع نسوة والمسألة بحالها يقع على كل واحدة طلقة بائنة وقيل تطلق واحدة منهن وإليه البيان وهو الأظهر والأشبه» يجب أن يكون معناه والمسألة بحالها يعني في التحريم لا بقيد أنت كما لا يخفى بل في هذا يحب أن لا يقع إلا على المخاطبة اهـ. ومثله في منح الغفار من بحث الصريح والشرنبلالية وفي العزمية على الدرر والغرر . كما يكون بمنزلة قوله هن طوالق لأن حلال الله يشملهن على سبيل الاستغراق لا على سبيل البدل في قوله إحداكن طالق، وحيث وقع الطلاق بهذا اللفظ وقع بائناً اهـ. ويوجد في بعض النسخ وفي الفتاوى وفي بعضها وفي الفتوى والأولى لا يدل على أنه أن هذا هو المفتى به مع القول هو المفتى به عند المتأخرين ولذا قال في البزازية ومشايخنا أفتوا في أنه لو قال أنت علي حرام والحلال عليه حرام أو حلال الله عليه حرام أو حلال المسلمين عليه حرام أن الكل بائن بلا نية وإذا حلف بهذه الألفاط على فعل في المستقبل ففعل وليست له امرأة عليه الكفارة، وإذا كان له امرأة وقت الحلف وماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط الصحيح أنه لا تطلق امرأته المتزوجة وعليه الفتوى لأن حلفه صار حلفاً بالله تعالى وقت الوجود فلا ينقلب طلاقاً خالعها ثم قال حلال الله علي حرام إن شرب إلى سنة وشرب لا يقع لعدم الملك والإضافة إليه، ولو قال لها إن تزوجتك فحلال الله علي حرام فتزوجها تطلق . قال بعضهم : والصحيح خلافه لوقوعه على القائمة لا على المتزوجة فلو لم تكن في نكاحه وقت وجود الشرط امرأة لا يقع على فلانة أيضاً وتمامه في البزازية. وفي قوله حلال الله عليه حرام وله امرأتان ولم تكن له نية طلقتا وإن نوى إحداهما دين لا في القضاء، وفتوى الإمام الاوزجندي على أنه يقع على واحدة وعليه البيان وقد ذكرناه. وفي الظهيرية : حلف بهذه الألفاظ أنه لم يفعل كذا وكان فعله وله امرأتان وأكثر بن، وإن ليست له امرأة فلا لم : ولعل مراد الزيلعي بكون المسألة بحالها هو أن يكون الحرام عنده طلاقاً، وأما كون المسألة في تلك الصورة على أن يقال أنت علي حرام فليس بداخل في ذلك فإن ما يقتضيه صحة المساق هو أن تكون العبارة ها هنا امرأتي علي حرام إذ لا مساغ لأن يقال لأربع نسوة أنت علي حرام ولا تتأتى صحة القولين المذكورين إلا على ما قررنا .اهـ.. لكن في قوله أن تكون العبارة ها هنا امرأتي علي حرام» نظر والظاهر إبداله بحلال الله أو حلال المسلمين لما ذكره المؤلف هنا عن الفتاوى من أن قوله امرأتي طالق وله أكثر من واحدة لا يقع إلا على واحدة، ولم يحكوا في هذا خلافاً بل ظاهر قوله بخلاف الصريح أنها اتفاقية كما ذكره في منح الغفار راداً على الدرر في ذكره التصحيح في الصريح أيضاً. وحينئذ فلا فرق فيما يظهر بين امرأتي طالق وبين امرأتي علي حرام في كونه لا يشمل غير واحدة فيما لو كان له أكثر إلا أن يوجد نقل بخلافه فيتبع فجعل العزمية محل الخلاف امرأتي حرام في كونه يقع على الكل أو على واحدة غير ظاهر. فالحاصل أن المخرج من محل النزاع أنت علي حرام كما مر وامرأتي علي حرام فتأمل وراجع وانظر في تعليل الفتح يتقوى عندك ما قلنا قوله: (ويوجد في بعض النسخ) أقول : يؤيد النسخة الثانية ما سيذكره المصنف. متناً في الأيمان كل حل عليه حرام فهو على الطعام والشراب، والفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته من غير نية. قال المؤلف هناك في شرحه لغلبة الاستعمال؛ كذا في الهداية . وإن لم تكن له امرأة ذكر في النهاية معزياً إلى النوازل أنه تجب عليه الكفارة اهـ. يعني
And upon them both [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], and according to them as he intended. He said to three [wives]: 'You are forbidden to me,' and he intended three for the first, and the oath for the second, and lying for the third, then she is divorced three times. And it is said that this is according to the second [Imam], and according to them it should be as he intended. That is it. His statement: (And in al-Fatawa: if he said to his wife 'You are forbidden to me' and al-haram according to him is divorce but he did not intend divorce, the divorce occurs) judicially because of what appeared from custom, such that even if he said to his wife 'If I marry you, then what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me' and he marries her, she is divorced. And for this reason none swear by it except men. We restricted it to the judicial because divorce does not occur religiously without intention. Imam Zahir al-Din mentioned: We do not say that intention is not required, but he is considered as intending customarily. If you say: When divorce occurs without intention, it should be like explicit [sarih] and so the occurring [divorce] should be raj'i, I say: The customary practice is to effect a ba'in divorce; thus in al-Bazaziyya. So if the author had said 'and it occurs as ba'in' it would have been better. And his statement 'You are with me in prohibition' is like his statement 'You are forbidden to me', and likewise his statement 'What the Muslims have made lawful is forbidden to me'. And in the places where divorce occurs with the word 'haram', if he has no woman [wife] then if he breaks [his oath], expiation is obligatory upon him. Al-Nasafi holds that it is not obligatory. And if he has more than one wife, he said in al-Fatawa: it falls on each one as a single divorce, unlike the explicit [phrase] which does not fall except as one when he says 'my wife is divorced' and he has more than one. And Shaykh al-Islam al-Auzjandi answered that it does not fall except on one, and to him is the clarification and it is the most similar; thus in al-Bazaziyya, al-Khulasa, and al-Dhakhira, and in Fath al-Qadir: and according to me, the most similar is what is in al-Fatawa because his statement 'What Allah has made lawful' or 'What the Muslims have made lawful' encompasses every wife. So if there is a custom in [such] use regarding divorce... (We restricted to the judicial etc.) I say: When it is joined to the explicit by custom, it does not need intention, but rather it needs the absence of intention of divorce that its wording can bear, as if he intended by 'you are divorced' [meaning] from bondage, as was explained at the beginning of divorce. His statement (I said: The customary practice is to effect a ba'in divorce) I say: This was the customary practice of their time, but in our time, most commoners who swear by it do not distinguish between ba'in and raj'i, let alone intending ba'in. So when it is like the explicit due to predominance in divorce, and we say it occurs without intention due to custom, it should occur as raj'i, so let it be considered. And it may be said: Even if it has become explicit by custom, its wording does not bear the occurrence of raj'i because that she is forbidden to him requires the illegality of approaching her, and the raj'i does not prohibit intercourse, as has passed, and it is not made ila' because it is prohibition while the marriage contract is in effect, and custom intends prohibition by divorce. And it does not contradict the occurrence of ba'in by it while it is explicit, because the explicit may sometimes yield ba'in, like a severe divorce, just as some kinayat yield raj'i, like 'consider yourself divorced' and 'seek to know your womb' and 'you are one'. So let it be considered. His statement: (And in Fath al-Qadir: and according to me, the most similar etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And I say: This is not complete in the statement 'You are forbidden to me' when addressing one, as the author said, and the commentator's statement 'and if he had four wives and the case as it is, it falls on each one as a ba'in divorce, and it is said that only one of them is divorced, and to him is the clarification and it is the most apparent and most similar' must mean that the case as it is means in prohibition, not with the restriction 'you', as is not hidden; rather in this it must not occur except on the addressed. End. And similar is in Manh al-Ghaffar from the discussion of sarih and al-Sharnablaliyya and in al-Azmiyya on al-Durar wa al-Ghurar. As it is like his statement 'They are all divorced' because 'what Allah has made lawful' encompasses them on the basis of inclusiveness, not on the basis of substitution like his statement 'One of you is divorced', and when divorce occurs by this wording, it occurs as ba'in. End. And it is found in some copies 'and in al-Fatawa' and in some copies 'and in al-Fatwa' and the first does not indicate that this is the mufta bihi, although the latter is the mufta bihi according to the later scholars. Therefore it is said in al-Bazaziyya: Our shaykhs issued fatwa that if he said 'You are forbidden to me' and 'what is lawful is forbidden to him' or 'what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to him' or 'what the Muslims have made lawful is forbidden to him', all are ba'in without intention. And if he swears by these phrases on a future action, then he does it, and he has no wife, then expiation is due upon him. And if he had a wife at the time of the oath, and she died before the condition or separated without a waiting period, then he fulfills the condition, the correct view is that his married wife is not divorced, and upon it is the fatwa, because his oath became an oath by Allah Almighty at the time of existence, so it does not turn into divorce. If he divorced her [khala'aha], then said 'What Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me if I drink for a year', and he drinks, it does not occur [as divorce] due to lack of ownership and attribution to her. And if he said to her 'If I marry you, then what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me', and he marries her, she is divorced. Some said: The correct is the opposite, because it occurs on the existing one, not on the one married. So if there was no woman in his marriage at the time of the condition's existence, it does not occur on such-and-such either. And its completion is in al-Bazaziyya. And in the statement 'What Allah has made lawful is forbidden to him' and he has two wives and he had no intention, both are divorced; and if he intended one of them, it is religiously [not judicially] [firm]. And the fatwa of Imam al-Auzjandi is that it falls on one, and the clarification has been mentioned. And in al-Zahiriyya: He swore by these words that he did not do such-and-such, and he did it, and he has two wives or more... and if he has no wife, then not... Perhaps al-Zayla'i's intent with 'the case as it is' is that haram according to him is divorce, and as for the case in that form that he says 'You are forbidden to me', that is not included in that, because what the correctness of the context requires is that the expression here should be 'My wife is forbidden to me', since it is not permissible to say to four women 'You are forbidden to me', and the correctness of the two mentioned statements is not possible except on what we have established. End. But in his statement 'the expression here should be 'My wife is forbidden to me'' there is consideration, and the apparent is to replace it with 'What Allah has made lawful' or 'What the Muslims have made lawful', because what the author mentioned here from al-Fatawa that his statement 'My wife is divorced' and he has more than one does not fall except on one, and they have not reported a difference in this, rather the apparent from his statement 'unlike the explicit' is that it is agreed upon, as mentioned in Manh al-Ghaffar in refutation of al-Durar in its mentioning the correction regarding the explicit as well. Therefore, there is no difference in what appears between 'My wife is divorced' and 'My wife is forbidden to me' in that it does not include more than one when he has more, unless a transmission to the contrary is found, then it is to be followed. So making al-Azmiyya the place of dispute about 'My wife is forbidden to me' concerning whether it falls on all or one is not apparent. So the summary is that the exit from the place of contention is 'You are forbidden to me' as has passed, and 'My wife is forbidden to me'. So reflect, refer, and look at the explanation of Fath al-Qadir, and what we said will become strong for you. His statement: (And it is found in some copies) I say: The second copy is supported by what the author will mention as matn in the chapter on oaths: 'Everything that is lawful is forbidden to him' it is regarding food and drink, and the fatwa is that his wife becomes separated from him without intention. The author said there in his commentary due to predominance of usage; similarly in al-Hidayah. And if he has no wife, it is mentioned in al-Nihaya citing al-Nawazil that expiation becomes obligatory upon him. End. Meaning
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling of saying 'anti 'alayya haram' (you are forbidden to me) in the context of divorce. The passage addresses multiple dimensions: the legal nature of the phrase, the requirement of intention, the difference between judicial (qada') and religious (diyanah) rulings, the type of divorce (ba'in vs. raj'i), and the effect when multiple wives are involved.

The text begins by continuing a discussion about a man saying 'antunna 'alayya haram' to three wives with specific intentions. It then moves to the main point: according to the fatwa, if a man says to his wife 'anti 'alayya haram' and in his understanding 'haram' means divorce, but he did not intend divorce, the divorce occurs judicially due to the prevalent custom. This is because the phrase has become so commonly used for divorce that it is treated like an explicit pronouncement (sarih). However, this is only in the judicial sphere (qada'); religiously (diyanah), divorce does not occur without intention. Imam Zahir al-Din explains that we do not say intention is unnecessary, but rather the man is deemed to have intended divorce by custom. When it becomes like sarih, intention is not needed, but one must ensure he did not intend something other than divorce that the wording could bear.

The commentary then discusses the type of divorce: the custom was to effect a ba'in divorce, so the ruling is that it falls as ba'in. However, the commentator notes that in his own time, commoners who use this phrase do not distinguish between ba'in and raj'i, and argues that since it has become like sarih due to custom, it should be raj'i. He reflects that the wording 'haram' implies complete prohibition, which seems to fit ba'in, yet some kinaya (indirect) phrases yield raj'i. He leaves this for consideration.

The passage then addresses the issue of multiple wives: if a man says 'halal Allah 'alayya haram' and has more than one wife, the fatwa in al-Fatawa states that it falls on each wife as one divorce, unlike the explicit 'my wife is divorced' which falls only on one. However, Shaykh al-Islam al-Auzjandi held that it falls only on one. The commentator in Fath al-Qadir prefers the fatwa because phrases like 'halal Allah' or 'halal al-muslimin' are general and cover all wives. But there is also a view that when addressing a specific wife, it only applies to her.

The text further discusses cases where the husband has no wife at the time of the oath: if he breaks the oath, expiation (kaffarah) is due. It also clarifies that if he had a wife at the time of swearing but she dies or separates before the condition, the ruling is that the new wife he marries later is not divorced; the oath is considered an oath by Allah and does not turn into a divorce.

Finally, the commentary mentions a difference in manuscripts regarding the phrase 'in al-Fatawa' vs 'in al-Fatwa', and notes that the relied-upon position among later scholars is that all such phrases (anti 'alayya haram, halal Allah, halal al-muslimin) effect a ba'in divorce without intention. The passage concludes by relating this to the chapter on oaths, where 'halal 'alayya haram' is interpreted as referring to food and drink unless the context indicates divorce.

Throughout, the commentator engages with earlier authorities like al-Bazaziyya, Fath al-Qadir, al-Nahr, and others, showing the evolution of the fatwa within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
حرام (haram): In this context, the word 'haram' (forbidden) is used as a pronouncement of divorce, either as a kinaya (indirect) or, due to custom, as a sarih (explicit) declaration.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without the possibility of revocation (ruju'). The customary divorce by 'haram' is ruled ba'in in the fatwa.
عرف (urf): Custom or prevailing practice. Urf is the basis for treating the kinaya as sarih in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi fatwa (mutakhkhirin), which view is preferred when a man with multiple wives says a general divorce phrase like 'halal Allah 'alayya haram'?
A) All wives are divorced.
B) Only one wife is divorced.
C) No divorce occurs because intention is required.
D) The divorce is suspensive until the husband clarifies.

Key Takeaway
When a general divorce phrase like 'halal Allah 'alayya haram' is used with multiple wives, the relied-upon later Hanafi view is that it divorces only one wife, not all.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Effect of 'Halal Allah Alayya Haram': Oath or Divorce?
Ruling on the phrase 'حلال الله علي حرام' as divorce or oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'Halal Allah alayya haram' and distinguish whether it constitutes an oath requiring kaffarah or an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) depending on the presence of a wife.

Core Idea
The phrase 'Halal Allah alayya haram' triggers ba'in divorce if a wife exists, but requires kaffarah if used as an oath without a wife.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the legal consequences of a common yet complex utterance: a man declaring 'the lawful of Allah is unlawful for me.' In Hanafi fiqh, such words can operate either as an implicit divorce or as an oath by Allah, depending on context. The commentator clarifies the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from later authorities, addressing scenarios with and without wives, multiple wives, and conditional phrases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its bearer
The phrase 'حلال الله علي حرام' or its variants. Determine whether it is addressed to a wife (present) or used as a general oath.
The utterance may be explicit or implied, e.g., 'you are unlawful to me.'
2
Determine the presence of a wife at the time of utterance
If a wife exists at the moment of speaking, the phrase is treated as a divorce (ba'in). If no wife is present, it is an oath by Allah.
The key is the marital status at the time of utterance, not later.
3
Analyze condition and subsequent events
If the utterance is conditional (e.g., 'if I do X') and the wife dies or separates before the condition is met, the oath reverts to an oath by Allah; a subsequent wife is not divorced.
The fatwa follows that the oath becomes an oath by Allah at the time of fulfillment.
4
Resolve intention in multiple wives
If the phrase is addressed to multiple wives without specific intention, all are divorced. If intention is specified (e.g., intending one), it is valid between him and Allah but not enforceable in court.
The court requires evidence of intention; the default is all are affected.
5
Note the threefold repetition ruling
Repeating the phrase three times with a condition results in three divorces.
This is consistent with the general rule on repeated divorce utterances.

كتاب الطلاق باب الإيلاء
كما يكون بمنزلة قوله هن طوالق لأن حلال الله يشملهن على سبيل الاستغراق لا على سبيل البدل في قوله إحداكن طالق، وحيث وقع الطلاق بهذا اللفظ وقع بائناً اهـ. ويوجد في بعض النسخ وفي الفتاوى وفي بعضها وفي الفتوى والأولى لا يدل على أنه أن هذا هو المفتى به مع القول هو المفتى به عند المتأخرين ولذا قال في البزازية ومشايخنا أفتوا في أنه لو قال أنت علي حرام والحلال عليه حرام أو حلال الله عليه حرام أو حلال المسلمين عليه حرام أن الكل بائن بلا نية وإذا حلف بهذه الألفاط على فعل في المستقبل ففعل وليست له امرأة عليه الكفارة، وإذا كان له امرأة وقت الحلف وماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط الصحيح أنه لا تطلق امرأته المتزوجة وعليه الفتوى لأن حلفه صار حلفاً بالله تعالى وقت الوجود فلا ينقلب طلاقاً خالعها ثم قال حلال الله علي حرام إن شرب إلى سنة وشرب لا يقع لعدم الملك والإضافة إليه، ولو قال لها إن تزوجتك فحلال الله علي حرام فتزوجها تطلق . قال بعضهم : والصحيح خلافه لوقوعه على القائمة لا على المتزوجة فلو لم تكن في نكاحه وقت وجود الشرط امرأة لا يقع على فلانة أيضاً وتمامه في البزازية. وفي قوله حلال الله عليه حرام وله امرأتان ولم تكن له نية طلقتا وإن نوى إحداهما دين لا في القضاء، وفتوى الإمام الاوزجندي على أنه يقع على واحدة وعليه البيان وقد ذكرناه. وفي الظهيرية : حلف بهذه الألفاظ أنه لم يفعل كذا وكان فعله وله امرأتان وأكثر بن، وإن ليست له امرأة فلا لم : ولعل مراد الزيلعي بكون المسألة بحالها هو أن يكون الحرام عنده طلاقاً، وأما كون المسألة في تلك الصورة على أن يقال أنت علي حرام فليس بداخل في ذلك فإن ما يقتضيه صحة المساق هو أن تكون العبارة ها هنا امرأتي علي حرام إذ لا مساغ لأن يقال لأربع نسوة أنت علي حرام ولا تتأتى صحة القولين المذكورين إلا على ما قررنا .اهـ.. لكن في قوله أن تكون العبارة ها هنا امرأتي علي حرام» نظر والظاهر إبداله بحلال الله أو حلال المسلمين لما ذكره المؤلف هنا عن الفتاوى من أن قوله امرأتي طالق وله أكثر من واحدة لا يقع إلا على واحدة، ولم يحكوا في هذا خلافاً بل ظاهر قوله بخلاف الصريح أنها اتفاقية كما ذكره في منح الغفار راداً على الدرر في ذكره التصحيح في الصريح أيضاً. وحينئذ فلا فرق فيما يظهر بين امرأتي طالق وبين امرأتي علي حرام في كونه لا يشمل غير واحدة فيما لو كان له أكثر إلا أن يوجد نقل بخلافه فيتبع فجعل العزمية محل الخلاف امرأتي حرام في كونه يقع على الكل أو على واحدة غير ظاهر. فالحاصل أن المخرج من محل النزاع أنت علي حرام كما مر وامرأتي علي حرام فتأمل وراجع وانظر في تعليل الفتح يتقوى عندك ما قلنا قوله: (ويوجد في بعض النسخ) أقول : يؤيد النسخة الثانية ما سيذكره المصنف. متناً في الأيمان كل حل عليه حرام فهو على الطعام والشراب، والفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته من غير نية. قال المؤلف هناك في شرحه لغلبة الاستعمال؛ كذا في الهداية . وإن لم تكن له امرأة ذكر في النهاية معزياً إلى النوازل أنه تجب عليه الكفارة اهـ. يعني

واذا كان له امرأة وقت الحلف إلى قوله فلا ينقلب طلاقاً) أقول: هكذا عبارة البزازية كما رأيته في نسختي والظاهر أن في عبارة البزازية سقطاً يدل عليه ما سيذكره المؤلف في الأيمان عن الظهيرية ونصه : وإن كانت له امرأة وقت اليمين فماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط لا تلزمه الكفارة لأن يمينه انصرف إلى الطلاق وقت وجودها، وإن لم تكن له امرأة وقت اليمين ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط اختلفوا فيه؛ قال الفقيه أبو جعفر : تبين المتزوجة. وقال غيره : لا تبين وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى لأن يمينه . جعل يميناً بالله تعالى وقت وجودها فلا يكون طلاقاً بعد ذلك اهـ. ومثله في الخانية .

قال ثلاث مرات حلال الله عليه حرام أن فعل كذا ووجد الشرط وقع الثلاث؛ كذا في البزازية والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Ila'. It is like his saying 'they are all divorced' because 'the lawful of Allah' includes them on the basis of generality, not on the basis of substitution in his saying 'one of you is divorced'. And where divorce occurs with this wording, it occurs as an irrevocable divorce (ba'in). End. And it is found in some copies and in al-Fatawa... and the fatwa is that if a man says 'you are unlawful to me' and 'the lawful is unlawful to him' or 'the lawful of Allah is unlawful to him' or 'the lawful of the Muslims is unlawful to him', that all are irrevocable divorce without intention. And if he swears by these words for a future action and does it, and he has no wife, he must expiate. And if he had a wife at the time of the oath and she died before the condition or left without a waiting period, then he fulfilled the condition, the correct view is that his subsequent wife is not divorced, and this is the fatwa because his oath became an oath by Allah at the time of existence, so it does not turn into divorce... And if he said 'every lawful is unlawful to me', it is about food and drink, and the fatwa is that his wife becomes divorced without intention. And if he had no wife, it is mentioned in al-Nihaya that he must expiate... And if he had a wife at the time of the oath... then the condition occurred, it is reported from al-Dhakhira that... And if he said 'the lawful of Allah is unlawful' three times and the condition occurred, three divorces occur; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And Allah is Most High and All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal rulings pertaining to the phrase 'حلال الله علي حرام' (the lawful of Allah is unlawful to me) and its variants. The commentator clarifies that such phrases, when uttered by a man, can constitute either an oath or a divorce depending on the context and intent. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) among later Hanafis is that if a man says 'أنت علي حرام' or 'حلال الله عليه حرام' without intending a specific number of divorces, it results in an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) without need for intention. However, if he utters such words as an oath for a future action and he has no wife at the time, he is obligated to perform expiation (kaffarah) upon violation. If he has a wife at the time of the oath but she dies or leaves before the condition is fulfilled, then upon fulfillment the subsequent wife is not divorced. The passage also addresses scenarios with multiple wives: if a husband says 'حلال الله عليه حرام' and has two wives without specifying his intention, both are divorced; if he intends one, it is between him and Allah but not enforceable in court. The commentator notes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether such phrases apply to all wives or just one, and provides the position of Imam al-Auzjandi that it falls on one wife with clarification. The passage ends with the ruling that repeating the phrase three times with a condition results in three divorces.

Key Terms
حلال الله علي حرام (halāl Allāh ʿalayya ḥarām): A phrase meaning 'the lawful of Allah is unlawful for me,' used as an oath or divorce wording.
بائن (bāʾin): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without the possibility of return except through a new contract.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for breaking an oath, typically feeding or clothing ten poor persons or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His wife (if he has one then) is divorced.
B) He must pay kaffarah (expiation).
C) Nothing is required.
D) He must perform hadd.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on 'حلال الله علي حرام' hinges on the presence of a wife at the time of utterance: it is a ba'in divorce if a wife exists, and an oath requiring kaffarah if not.

Definition and Ruling of Khul' (Divorce by Mutual Consent)
Defining Khul' with its legal effect and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Khul' in Hanafi fiqh, state its legal ruling (mubah), list its essential pillars and conditions, and explain why it results in a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce.

Core Idea
Khul' is a separation by the husband accepting compensation from the wife, resulting in an irrevocable divorce.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, Khul' is a distinct form of marital dissolution where the wife offers compensation in exchange for her release. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines Khul' both linguistically and legally, positions it among other forms of divorce, and outlines the essential elements that make it valid. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing Khul' from regular talaq and for correctly applying the rulings that flow from it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
The passage gives both the linguistic meaning (extraction) and the Shari'ah definition: separation from marriage occurring by khul' or by divorce for wealth, resulting in ba'in talaq. Note that the legal definition includes the element of compensation and the irrevocable nature.
The Hanafi definition emphasizes that khul' is a compound act: the husband's pronouncement and the wife's payment.
2
Determine the hukm (legal ruling) of Khul'
Khul' is generally permissible (mubah) but can be makruh if done without valid reason. The passage states that it is disliked (kariha) for the husband to demand anything beyond the agreed sum, as this would turn a permissible act into a sinful one.
The ruling is derived from the general principle that contracts are allowed unless prohibited.
3
List the pillars (arkan) and conditions (shurut) of a valid Khul'
From the passage and standard Hanafi fiqh, identify the necessary elements: capacity of parties (adult, sane), offer and acceptance, consideration (mal) that is lawful, and clear wording (e.g., 'khala'tuki' or 'taliqi'). Conditions include mutual consent and the wife being free from duress.
The passage does not enumerate all conditions explicitly, so we rely on the established school position.
4
Understand the legal effect: ba'in divorce
Khul' results in an irrevocable divorce (ba'in). The husband cannot take back his wife during iddah; a new marriage contract is required. The wife must pay the agreed compensation, and it becomes a debt on her.
This distinguishes Khul' from a revocable (raj'i) talaq, where the husband may return to his wife without a new contract.
5
Analyze the ancillary issue: dislike of demanding more
The passage explains that it is disliked to demand anything beyond the agreed sum because if the husband intends divorce, he should not seek wealth; if he intends an oath, it is a false oath. This highlights the importance of intention (niyyah) in these transactions.
This ruling discourages extortionate demands and protects the wife from exploitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الخلع
كانت لما اشترك مع الإيلاء في أن كلاً منهما قد يكون معصية وقد يكون مباحاً وزاد الخلع عليه بتسمية المال أخر عنه لأنه بمنزلة المركب من المفرد وقدما على الظهار واللعان لأنهما لا ينفكان عن المعصية. وهو لغة النزع يقال خلعت النعل وغيره خلعاً نزعته، وخالعت المرأة زوجها. وشرعاً: هو الفصل من النكاح الواقع به وبالطلاق على مال طلاق بائن ولزمها المال وكره شيء عليه لأنه إن حمل على الطلاق فلا يراد به شيء آخر، وإن حمل على اليمين فهو غموس . وفي فوائد شيخ الإسلام قال حلال الله عليه حرام إن فعل كذا وفعله وحلف بطلاق امرأته إن فعل كذا وفعله وله امرأتان فأراد أن يصرف هذين الطلاقين في واحدة منهما أشار في الزيادات إلى أنه يملك وذلك وفي الذخيرة : إن فعل كذا فحلال الله عليه حرام ثم حلف كذلك على فعل آخر وحنث في الأول ووقع الطلاق على امرأته ثم حنث في اليمين الثانية وهي في العدة؛ قيل لا يقع والأشبه الوقوع لالتحاق البائن بالبائن إذا كان معلقاً. قالت أنا عليك حرام فقال لا أدري أحلال أم حرام لا يقع شيء قال بين يدي أصحابه من امرأته عليه حراماً فليفعل هذا الأمر ففعله واحد منهم قال في المحيط : هذا إقرار منه بحرمتها عليه في الحكم. وقيل لا يكون إقراراً بالحرمة. قال ثلاث مرات حلال الله عليه حرام أن فعل كذا ووجد الشرط وقع الثلاث؛ كذا في البزازية والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.

ترك المؤلف من عبارة المتن قوله هو الفصل من النكاح ولعله ساقط من نسخته قوله : (ويرد
Chapter of Khul'. It was placed after Ila' because they both may be sin or permissible, and Khul' adds the specification of wealth, so it was delayed because it is like a compound of parts. It was placed before Zihar and Li'an because they are not freed from sin. In language, it means extraction: one says 'khala'tu al-na'la' (I removed the shoe) etc., and a woman 'khala'at' her husband. In Shari'ah: it is separation from marriage occurring by it and by divorce for wealth, an irrevocable divorce, and the wealth becomes obligatory for her, and it is disliked to demand anything from her because if it is interpreted as divorce, nothing else is intended; if interpreted as an oath, it is false. And in Fawa'id Shaykh al-Islam: he said 'the lawful of Allah is unlawful' if he does such-and-such, and he did it, and he swore by the divorce of his wife if he does such-and-such, and he did it, and he had two wives, and he wanted to direct these two divorces to one of them, it is indicated in al-Ziyadat that he may do so... And in al-Dhakhirah: if he swore 'the lawful of Allah is unlawful' on one action, then similarly on another, and he broke the first, divorce occurred, then he broke the second while she was in her 'iddah; it was said it does not occur, but the more likely is occurrence because one irrevocable divorce is attached to another when suspended. If she said 'I am unlawful to you' and he said 'I do not know whether lawful or unlawful', nothing occurs. He said in the presence of his companions: '[if] from his wife it is unlawful for him, let him do this thing,' so one of them did it. He said in al-Muhit: this is an acknowledgment of her unlawfulness to him in legal effect. And it was said it is not an acknowledgment of unlawfulness. He said: three times 'the lawful of Allah is unlawful' if he does such-and-such and the condition occurs, three [divorces] occur; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And Allah is Most High and All-Knowing. The author omitted from the text his saying 'it is the separation from marriage' and perhaps it is missing from his copy. He said: (and it is refuted).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Khul' (divorce by mutual consent with compensation). The commentator explains why Khul' is placed after Ila' and before Zihar and Li'an: Khul' involves a financial element, making it more complex, while Ila' and Khul' can be either sinful or permissible, whereas Zihar and Li'an are always sinful. The definition of Khul' is given both linguistically (extraction/removal) and legally: it is a separation from marriage that occurs through the wording of Khul' or through divorce for a consideration, resulting in an irrevocable divorce (ba'in). The wife must pay the agreed compensation, and it is disliked for the husband to demand anything beyond that because if he intends divorce, he should not seek wealth; if he intends an oath, it is a false oath. The passage also discusses related issues: if a man says 'حلال الله عليه حرام' as an oath and then swears similarly again, and the first condition is broken causing divorce, the second oath while she is in iddah may also trigger divorce. If the wife says 'I am unlawful to you' and the husband responds ambiguously, no divorce occurs. A story illustrates that if a man acknowledges his wife is unlawful to him by his action, it may be considered an acknowledgment. The passage concludes that repeating 'حلال الله عليه حرام' three times with a condition results in triple divorce. The final note indicates that the author omitted a phrase from the matn, possibly due to a missing copy.

Key Terms
خلع (khulʿ): A separation from marriage by the husband accepting compensation from the wife, resulting in an irrevocable divorce.
بائن (bāʾin): An irrevocable divorce; after Khul', the husband cannot take back his wife except with a new marriage contract.
مال (māl): Wealth or consideration given by the wife to the husband in exchange for the divorce in Khul'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The husband can take back his wife within the iddah.
C) It results in an irrevocable divorce (ba'in talaq).
D) It is a form of talaq with the right of return.

Key Takeaway
Khul' is a valid form of separation where the wife pays compensation, resulting in a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce.

Rulings on 'Halal Allah 'Alayhi Haram' and Related Conditional Divorce Phrases
Explains rulings on conditional divorce phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on the phrase 'halal Allah 'alayhi haram' in various scenarios including intention, multiple wives, consecutive oaths, and the wife's statement, and distinguish between talaq and oath effects.

Core Idea
The phrase 'halal Allah 'alayhi haram' functions as talaq if intended, and its effect can be directed to one wife; subsequent oaths during iddah may or may not fall.

This lesson delves into the nuanced rulings of conditional divorce phrases, particularly 'halal Allah 'alayhi haram' and similar expressions. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the speaker's intention—whether as an oath or as talaq—and provides detailed guidance for scenarios involving multiple wives, consecutive oaths, and uncertainty. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying talaq law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its legal classification
Recognize that phrases like 'halal Allah 'alayhi haram' can be either an oath (yamin) or a pronouncement of talaq depending on the speaker's intention. If intended as talaq, it falls as talaq; if as an oath, it is a sinful false oath (yamin ghamus).
The default is not automatically talaq; intention is key.
2
Determine the role of intention in the ruling
Apply the principle that utterances are interpreted according to intention. If the husband intends talaq, the phrase operates as one talaq; otherwise it is an oath requiring expiation.
This is consistent with the Hanafi maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.
3
Apply the rule for multiple wives
If the husband has two wives and swears by talaq of his wife (using such phrases) then fulfills the condition, he may direct both divorces to one of the wives. This is indicated in al-Ziyadat.
The husband has discretion to specify which wife is divorced.
4
Analyze consecutive oaths during iddah
If the first conditional oath is broken and talaq falls, then a second similar oath is broken while the wife is in iddah, there is a dispute: some say it does not fall, but the more likely (ashbah) view is that it falls because the first ba'in divorce joins the second conditional ba'in.
This is an example of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Consider the wife's statement and triple repetition
If the wife says 'I am haram for you' and the husband responds 'I don't know', no talaq occurs. If the husband says 'if one's wife is haram for him, let him do this' and someone does it, al-Muhit says this is an acknowledgment of prohibition in legal effect (though another view disagrees). Saying the phrase three times results in three divorces.
The triple repetition rule is clear: three utterances = three divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فوائد شيخ الإسلام قال حلال الله عليه حرام إن فعل كذا وفعله وحلف بطلاق امرأته إن فعل كذا وفعله وله امرأتان فأراد أن يصرف هذين الطلاقين في واحدة منهما أشار في الزيادات إلى أنه يملك وذلك وفي الذخيرة : إن فعل كذا فحلال الله عليه حرام ثم حلف كذلك على فعل آخر وحنث في الأول ووقع الطلاق على امرأته ثم حنث في اليمين الثانية وهي في العدة؛ قيل لا يقع والأشبه الوقوع لالتحاق البائن بالبائن إذا كان معلقاً. قالت أنا عليك حرام فقال لا أدري أحلال أم حرام لا يقع شيء قال بين يدي أصحابه من امرأته عليه حراماً فليفعل هذا الأمر ففعله واحد منهم قال في المحيط : هذا إقرار منه بحرمتها عليه في الحكم. وقيل لا يكون إقراراً بالحرمة. قال ثلاث مرات حلال الله عليه حرام أن فعل كذا ووجد الشرط وقع الثلاث؛ كذا في البزازية والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
And in Fawa'id Shaykh al-Islam: He said 'halal Allah 'alayhi haram if he does such and such' and he did it, and he swore by his wife's talaq that he would do such and such, and he did it, and he had two wives and wanted to assign both divorces to one of them; al-Ziyadat indicates that he has that right. And in al-Dhakhira: If he does such and such, then halal Allah 'alayhi haram, then he swore similarly on another action and broke the first, and talaq fell on his wife; then he broke the second oath while she was in 'iddah; it is said it does not fall, but the more likely is that it falls because one ba'in joins another ba'in if conditional. She said: 'I am haram for you,' and he said, 'I do not know whether it is halal or haram,' nothing falls. He said in the presence of his companions: 'If one's wife is haram for him, let him do this matter' and one of them did it. Al-Muhit: This is an acknowledgment of her being haram for him in legal effect. And it is said it is not an acknowledgment of prohibition. He said three times: 'Halal Allah 'alayhi haram if he does such and such,' and the condition was found, the three divorces fell. Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage addresses various scenarios where a man uses phrases like 'halal Allah 'alayhi haram' (what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me) or 'anti 'alayya haram' (you are forbidden to me) as conditional statements. The discussion covers cases where the condition is fulfilled and the subsequent rulings. If the man intends talaq by such phrases, it operates as talaq; if he intends an oath, it is a sinful oath. The text also discusses the situation of having two wives and wanting to direct both divorces to one wife, citing al-Ziyadat that he has that right. Further, it considers the case of a wife saying 'I am haram for you' and the husband being unsure—in that case, no talaq occurs. The passage also relays an opinion from al-Muhit that the husband's statement 'if one's wife is haram for him, let him do this matter' is an acknowledgment of the prohibition in legal effect, though another view disagrees. Finally, it rules that saying it three times results in three divorces.

Key Terms
حلال الله عليه حرام (halal Allah 'alayhi haram): An utterance meaning 'what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me', treated as a yamin (oath) or talaq depending on intention.
اليمين الغموس (al-yamin al-ghamus): A deliberate false oath about a past event.
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that severs the marriage tie immediately; the wife must observe iddah but can remarry after.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One talaq falls.
C) It is treated as zihar.
D) Nothing occurs; it is a mere statement.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'halal Allah 'alayhi haram' is a multi-purpose utterance whose legal effect depends on intention; when intended as talaq, it operates as such, and additional rules apply for multiple wives, consecutive oaths, and repetition.

The Definition of Khul' in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining khul' and comparing definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of khul' and explain why it is preferred over alternative definitions.

Core Idea
Khul' is the removal of marital ownership dependent on the wife's acceptance, not simply a divorce for compensation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the Hanafi definition of khul' and defends it against other definitions. You will learn the precise wording and the reason behind each element.

Guided Path
1
Extract the linguistic definition
Understand the metaphor of removing a garment because spouses are garments for each other.
2
Identify the legal definition
The author's chosen definition: إزالة ملك النكاح المتوقفة على قبولها بلفظ الخلع أو ما في معناه (removal of marital ownership, dependent on her acceptance, with the word khul' or its synonyms).
3
Analyze the rejection of alternative definitions
See why definitions that require compensation or that ignore the conditional acceptance are insufficient.
4
Examine the compensation dispute
Note the difference between the author's definition (not requiring specified compensation) and Abu Yusuf's view that khul' is only with compensation.
5
Apply the definition
Recognize that even without specifying compensation, khul' is valid and drops rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الخلع ١١٩ زوجها مخالعة إذا افتدت منه وطلقها على الفدية فخلعها هو خلعاً والاسم الخلع بالضم، وهو . استعارة من خلع اللباس لأن كل واحد منهما لباس للآخر فإذا فعلا ذلك فكان كل واحد نزع لباسه عنه ؛ كذا في المصباح وشرعاً على ما اخترناه إزالة ملك النكاح المتوقفة على قبولها بلفظ الخلع أو ما في معناه وقولي هذا أولى من قول بعض الشارحين أخذه المال بإزاء ملك النكاح لمغايرته المفهوم اللغوي من كل وجه والأصل أن يتحد جنس المفهومين، ويزاد في الشرعي قيد لإخراج اللغوي ولأنه يرد عليه الطلاق على مال وليس مساوياً له في جميع أحكامه لاستقلال حكم الخلع باسقاط الحقوق وإن اشتركا في البينونة. ويرد عليه أيضاً ما إذا عري عن البدل كما سنذكره. وقولي أيضاً أولى مما اختاره في فتح القدير من أنه إزالة ملك النكاح ببدل بلفظ الخلع لأنه يرد عليه ما إذا قال خالعتك ولم يسم . شيئاً فقبلت فإنه خلع مسقط للحقوق ـ كما في الخلاصة - إلا أن يقال مهرها الذي سقط به بدل فلم يعر عن البدل. فإن قلت: لو كانت قبضت جميع المهر ما حكمه؟ قلت ذكر قاضيخان أنها تردّ عليه ما ساق إليها من الصداق كما ذكره الحاكم الشهيد في المختصر وخواهر زاده وأخذ به ابن الفضل، قال القاضي : وهذا يؤيد ما ذكرنا عن أبي يوسف أن الخلع لا يكون إلا بعوض اهـ. وسيأتي تمامه آخر الباب. وإنما قيدنا بالمفاعلة لأنه لو قال خلعتك ناوياً وقع بائناً غير مسقط كما سيأتي وهو خارج عن تعريفنا بقولنا المتوقفة على قبولها لعدم توقفه كما في الخلاصة. ويرد عليه أيضاً ما إذا كان بلفظ المباراة فإنه يقع به البائن وتسقط الحقوق كالخلع بلفظه وما إذا كان بلفظ البيع والشراء فإنه خلع مسقط للحقوق على ما صححه في الصغرى وإن صرح قاضيخان بخلافه فلذا زدنا في تعريفنا أو ما في معناه. واستفيد من قولنا «إزالة ملك النكاح أنه لو خالع المطلقة رجعياً بمال فإنه يصح ويجب المال، ولو خالعها بمال ثم خالعها كما لم يصح في القنية ولكن يحتاج إلى الفرق بين ما إذا خالعها بعد الخلع حيث لم يصح، وبين ما إذا طلقها بمال بعد الخلع حيث يقع ولا يجب المال وقد ذكرناه في آخر عليه أيضاً) أي على ما في الفتح قال في النهر : من تأمل قوله في الفتح الطلاق على مال ليس هو الخلع بل في حكمه لا مطلقاً وإلا لجرى فيه الخلاف في أنه فسخ وفي سقوط المهر علم أن المباراة من ألفاظ الخلع، وأما الخلع بلفظ البيع والشراء فلا یری ما في الخانية اهـ. ونقل في حاشية مسكين عن شيخه أن هذه العبارة غير موجودة في خط صاحب النهر والموجود فيه وأقول : لا حاجة إلى ما زيد إذ المباراة ليست خلعاً بل كالخلع في حكمه على ما ستعرفه اهـ قوله : (لكن يحتاج إلى الفرق الخ) أقول : الفرق ظاهر وهو أن الخلع بعد الخلع لم يصح لأن البائن لا يلحق البائن أما الطلاق بمال بعد الخلع إنما صح لأنها بالخلع بانت منه والطلاق بمال لا يفيد البينونة لحصولها قبله، والمال إنما يلزم بمقابلة ملكها نفسها فإذا كانت مالكة نفسها بالخلع لم يلزم المال لعدم ما يقتضي لزومه فيقع في العدة يرد لأنه الكنايات. وخرج الخلع بعد الطلاق البائن وبعد الردة فإنه غير صحيح فيهما فلا يسقط المهر ويبقى له بعد الخلع ولاية الجبر على النكاح في الردة كما في البزازية .
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul'. The husband makes khul' with his wife if she ransoms herself from him and he divorces her for a ransom, so he makes khul' with her. The noun is khul' (ضم). It is a metaphor from removing a garment because each of them is a garment for the other; when they do that, each has removed their garment from the other. So in al-Misbah. Legally, according to our chosen view, it is the removal of marital ownership, dependent on her acceptance, with the word khul' or its synonyms. This statement is better than the statement of some commentators: taking wealth in exchange for marital ownership, because it differs from the linguistic meaning completely. The principle is that the two meanings should be of the same kind, and the legal definition is increased by a condition to exclude the linguistic meaning. And because it is objected that divorce for wealth is not equal to it in all its rulings, as the ruling of khul' is independent in dropping rights, although they share in causing irrevocability. Also it is objected if it is without compensation, as we will mention. My statement is also better than what al-Fath al-Qadeer chose: that it is the removal of marital ownership with compensation by the word khul', because it is objected if he says 'I make khul' with you' without specifying anything and she accepts, then it is a khul' that drops rights, as in al-Khulasa. Except if it is said her mahr which is dropped is compensation, so it is not devoid of compensation. If you say: if she had received all the mahr, what is the ruling? I say: Qadi Khan mentioned that she returns to him what he gave her as dowry, as al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned in al-Mukhtasar and Khaharzadah, and Ibn al-Fadl adopted it. Al-Qadi said: This supports what we mentioned from Abu Yusuf that khul' is only with compensation. The completion will come at the end of the chapter. We restricted it with the form of mutual exchange because if he says 'I make khul' with you' intending it, it occurs as bain but not dropping rights, as will come, and it is outside our definition by our statement 'dependent on her acceptance' because it is not dependent, as in al-Khulasa. Also objected is if it is with the word mubara'ah, for it occurs as bain and drops rights, like khul' with its wording, and if it is with the word bay' and shira', then it is a khul' that drops rights, according to what is authenticated in al-Sughra, although Qadi Khan stated otherwise. That is why we added to our definition 'or its synonyms'. It is understood from our statement 'removal of marital ownership' that if he makes khul' with a revocably divorced woman for wealth, it is valid and the wealth is obligatory. If he makes khul' with her for wealth and then makes khul' with her again, it is not valid as in al-Quniya, but one needs to distinguish between when he makes khul' after khul' (not valid) and when he divorces her for wealth after khul' (valid but wealth not obligatory). We have mentioned it at the end. Also) regarding what is in al-Fath, al-Nahr said: He who contemplates al-Fath's statement that divorce for wealth is not khul' but is in its ruling not absolutely—otherwise the disagreement whether it is an annulment and dropping of mahr would apply—knows that mubara'ah is from the words of khul'. As for khul' with the word bay' and shira', I do not see it in al-Khaniya. And it was transmitted in Hashiyat Miskin from his shaykh that this statement does not exist in the handwriting of the author of al-Nahr; what exists is: I say: There is no need for what was added, for mubara'ah is not khul' but like khul' in its ruling, as you will know. His statement: (But one needs to distinguish etc.) I say: The distinction is clear: khul' after khul' is not valid because a bain does not attach to a bain. As for divorce for wealth after khul', it is valid because she became bain from him through khul', and divorce for wealth does not bring about bain since it occurred before; the wealth is only obligatory in exchange for her ownership of herself. So if she owns herself through khul', the wealth is not obligatory due to the lack of what necessitates its obligation, so it falls. In the 'iddah, it is rejected because... the kinayat. And khul' after bain divorce and after apostasy is not valid in both, so the mahr does not drop, and he retains the guardianship of compulsion over marriage in apostasy after khul', as in al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a definition and legal analysis of khul'. The author first gives the linguistic definition: khul' is a metaphor from removing a garment because spouses are garments for each other. Then he gives the legal definition: in the Hanafi school, khul' is the removal of marital ownership, conditional upon the wife's acceptance, using the word khul' or its synonyms. He argues that this definition is better than two other definitions: (1) taking wealth in exchange for marital ownership, because that does not match the linguistic meaning and because it excludes cases without compensation; and (2) the definition from Fath al-Qadeer that includes compensation, because it fails to account for the case where the husband says 'I make khul' with you' without specifying anything—that still counts as khul' that drops rights, as per al-Khulasa. The author then addresses a potential objection: if the wife had already taken her entire mahr, what happens? He quotes Qadi Khan that she returns the dowry, supporting Abu Yusuf's view that khul' requires compensation. He then explains why he used the form of mutual exchange: if the husband says 'khala'tuka' intending it without the wife's acceptance, it still causes bain but does not drop rights, so it is outside the definition. He also clarifies that the word mubara'ah and bay'/shira' are included in the synonyms of khul', and that khul' after a revocable divorce is valid. He distinguishes between khul' after khul' (invalid) and divorce for wealth after khul' (valid but no money, because she already owns herself). Finally, he states that khul' after a bain divorce or after wife's apostasy is invalid altogether.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Removal of marital ownership dependent on wife's acceptance with the word khul' or its synonyms.
المباراة (al-mubāra’ah): A synonym for khul' that also results in bain divorce and dropping of rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the author's chosen definition and the definition from Fath al-Qadeer?
A) The author requires the word 'khul'', while Fath al-Qadeer allows any divorce wording.
B) The author's definition does not require compensation, while Fath al-Qadeer's definition does.
C) The author includes mubara'ah as a synonym; Fath al-Qadeer excludes it.
D) The author's definition requires the wife's acceptance; Fath al-Qadeer's does not.

Key Takeaway
Khul' is a form of divorce initiated by the husband but requiring the wife's acceptance, and it does not necessarily require specified compensation.

Conditions and Validity of Khul'
Explaining conditions and validity scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions for a valid khul' and identify cases where khul' is invalid.

Core Idea
Khul' requires the husband's offer and wife's acceptance using specific words; it is invalid after bain divorce or apostasy.

This portion of the text explains the specific conditions for khul' to be valid and its effect in various scenarios, including after revocable divorce, after a prior khul', after bain divorce, and after apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement of mutual form
The author used the form of mutual exchange (مفاعلة) because a unilateral statement does not require acceptance.
2
List the required words
Khul', mubara'ah, bay', shira' are all valid words for khul'.
3
Apply the definition to revocable divorce
Khul' after a revocable divorce is valid and the compensation is owed.
4
Distinguish between khul' after khul' (invalid) and divorce for wealth after khul' (valid but no money)
Khul' after khul' is invalid because a bain divorce cannot be followed by another bain; but divorce for wealth after khul' is valid because she is already bain, but no money is owed because she owns herself.
5
Determine invalid cases
Khul' after bain divorce or apostasy is invalid, and the mahr is not dropped.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الخلع ١١٩ زوجها مخالعة إذا افتدت منه وطلقها على الفدية فخلعها هو خلعاً والاسم الخلع بالضم، وهو . استعارة من خلع اللباس لأن كل واحد منهما لباس للآخر فإذا فعلا ذلك فكان كل واحد نزع لباسه عنه ؛ كذا في المصباح وشرعاً على ما اخترناه إزالة ملك النكاح المتوقفة على قبولها بلفظ الخلع أو ما في معناه وقولي هذا أولى من قول بعض الشارحين أخذه المال بإزاء ملك النكاح لمغايرته المفهوم اللغوي من كل وجه والأصل أن يتحد جنس المفهومين، ويزاد في الشرعي قيد لإخراج اللغوي ولأنه يرد عليه الطلاق على مال وليس مساوياً له في جميع أحكامه لاستقلال حكم الخلع باسقاط الحقوق وإن اشتركا في البينونة. ويرد عليه أيضاً ما إذا عري عن البدل كما سنذكره. وقولي أيضاً أولى مما اختاره في فتح القدير من أنه إزالة ملك النكاح ببدل بلفظ الخلع لأنه يرد عليه ما إذا قال خالعتك ولم يسم . شيئاً فقبلت فإنه خلع مسقط للحقوق ـ كما في الخلاصة - Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Removal of marital ownership dependent on wife's acceptance with the word khul' or its synonyms.
المباراة (al-mubāra’ah): A synonym for khul' that also results in bain divorce and dropping of rights.
البائن (al-bā'in): An irrevocable divorce; the marriage cannot be revived without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following scenarios renders khul' invalid?
A) Khul' during the wife's menstruation.
B) Khul' after a revocable divorce (during iddah).
C) Khul' after a bain divorce.
D) Khul' without specifying compensation.

Key Takeaway
Khul' requires a valid marriage; it is invalid after an irrevocable (bain) divorce or after the wife's apostasy.
